Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Complete Definition
Author challenges logicians' claim that complete definition requires indication of all essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya’s argument that the logicians’ complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) fails to indicate all essential attributes through direct conformity (mutabaqa), relying instead on connotation (iltizam).

Core Idea
The logicians' complete definition cannot deliver its promise of full quiddity knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that a complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm) reveals the full quiddity of a thing and distinguishes it from all others. In this passage, he dissects the standard definition 'insan hayawan natiq' to show that the genus term 'hayawan' does not directly indicate the essential attributes of growth, sensation, and voluntary motion; instead, it only hints at them by connotation. This undermines the epistemological pretensions of logicians who claim definitions yield certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find the assertion that a complete definition must depict the quiddity and distinguish it by indicating all essential attributes. Understand what the logicians promise.
2
Distinguish mutabaqa from iltizam
Mutabaqa is direct correspondence between a term and its meaning (e.g., 'human' directly indicates the species). Iltizam is connotation: one term implies another by necessity (e.g., 'rational' implies 'animal'). Grasp this distinction.
These are key concepts in Arabic semantics and logic.
3
Apply the distinction to 'insan hayawan natiq'
Examine the definition. The differentia 'natiq' (rational) indicates 'animal' by iltizam, not mutabaqa. Similarly, 'hayawan' should indicate 'growth', 'sensation', etc. by mutabaqa if the definition is complete, but it does not.
4
Recognize the failure
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the genus term 'hayawan' fails to directly indicate all the essential attributes of the genus. The logicians cannot provide a word that covers all these attributes by mutabaqa. Thus the definition is incomplete by their own criteria.
5
Generalize the critique
Understand that this critique applies to any 'complete definition' that relies on a genus term to subsume multiple essential attributes. The logicians' project of certain knowledge through definitions is therefore flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم ان الحد التام تقيا تصوير الحقيقة وتمييزها عما يكون مول من الذاتي المميز والثاني المشرك وهو التعريف هل تشترطون فيه ان تتصور جميع صفاته الذاتية التي تميز بها بينه وبين غيره أم لا فان الشرطم دلك لزمان تقولو ا سلا تقسیم نامی حساس متحرك بالاراده فاما لفظ الحيوان ولاية العلى هذه الصفات بالمطابقة ولا يطها وان در تبر طوان البرد واكتفوا عمود العيد المية كالمناطق مثلا فان الناطق بدل على الحيوان كنا. نه لا الحيوان على النامي اد النامي حتى قديد الحيوان سرك فيه للديوان والنات مادا اردت والعنوان يوان على ومعهور مل ان في الكناس المحرك نالاراده كما تقول الانسان حيوان ناطق وللقصود الحمران اكتفوا في الدلاله على الصفات المتبركه نمايد، لك بالتحمر و او بالالتزام فالفعل بدل على ذلك وان ارادوا تفصلنا سيد لاله المطابقة فلم يعلوا ذلك وهناي ان احالهم في الحد العام الجنس الغريب دون غير و نظر هذا دعواهم از الرمان همدان کون مولعا مر مقل قصر لا انار والا کر فاز اکثر واحد قالوا الاخرى محد وفر و سمن مياسر الضمير و از اهم نه لاصقدمات بالواهن ـ متلام ما من المواعى الملابد احد من الطير من وفصل ولا يده العباس من تفسير و كليد لام النقي في مقدمه وبدلاتم العمل متر و مولات الاسلام اندر على المنزل ولفظ بدل على المنزوزحمهم الأعداد
Their statement that the complete definition truly pictures the quiddity and distinguishes it from what is composed of the distinguishing essential and the second common [attribute] - do you require in it that you conceive all its essential attributes by which it is distinguished from other things or not? For if you require that, you would be saying [something like] a division of a growing, sensitive, moving by will. So the word 'animal' indicates these attributes by conformity (mutabaqa) or not? And if you only require the differentia like 'rational' for example, then 'rational' indicates 'animal' by connotation (iltizam), not 'animal' indicates 'growing' etc. So they are content in indicating the attributes with connotation or by implication. And if they wanted a detailed indication by conformity, they could not do that. And their failure in the common definition [is] the accidental genus without other. And consider their claim ...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' concept of 'complete definition' (al-hadd al-tamm). He attacks the claim that a complete definition must indicate all essential attributes of a quiddity. The author points out that when the logicians define 'human' as 'rational animal', the word 'animal' does not indicate all attributes of the genus (like growth, sensation, voluntary motion) by direct conformity (mutabaqa); rather, these attributes are only indirectly indicated via connotation (iltizam) or implication. If they required full detailed indication, they would find it impossible. This shows their definition fails to meet their own standards. Ibn Taymiyya thus exposes a self-contradiction in their epistemological framework: they claim complete definitions give full knowledge, but they cannot actually deliver it.

Key Terms
الحد التام (al-hadd al-tamm): The complete definition that supposedly reveals the quiddity and distinguishes it from all else by listing all essential attributes. [Note: In standard logic, al-hadd al-tamm is the definition by genus and differentia; Ibn Taymiyya here critiques its epistemological pretensions.]
المطابقة (al-mutabaqa): Indication by direct correspondence between a word and the meaning it signifies. [Note: Stable term across logic and linguistics.]
الالتزام (al-iltizam): Connotation or necessary implication, where one term implies another by logical necessity. [Note: Stable term, but sometimes distinguished from التضمن (partial implication).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, why does the definition 'insan hayawan natiq' fail to indicate all essential attributes of 'insan'?
A) Because 'natiq' is not an essential attribute.
B) Because 'hayawan' does not indicate growth, sensation, and voluntary motion by direct conformity (mutabaqa).
C) Because the definition is too short.
D) Because 'natiq' indicates 'hayawan' by conformity (mutabaqa).

Key Takeaway
The logicians' complete definition does not deliver certain knowledge of quiddities because the genus term cannot directly indicate all essential attributes; it relies on connotation, which is insufficient for the epistemological claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition 'al-salah: aqwal wa af'al mufattaha bi al-takbir wa mukhtatama bi al-taslim' (prayer: words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim). Check whether the genus 'aqwal wa af'al' indicates all the essential attributes of salah by mutabaqa or only by iltizam. Write a short analysis using Ibn Taymiyya's method.
?
Identify a definition from kalam (e.g., 'al-jawhar: al-qā'im bi nafsihi' or substance as self-subsisting). Evaluate whether the differentia 'al-qā'im bi nafsihi' indicates the genus by mutabaqa or iltizam, and discuss the implications for the certainty of the definition.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Definitions Without Evidence
Ibn Taymiyya argues that a definition accepted on authority alone is unreliable and cannot produce knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that a definition without evidence is epistemically equivalent to a khabar al-wahid, and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
A definition from authority alone is epistemically worthless — it is just a single report.

Logicians often define terms without providing evidence for their definitions. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this by drawing an analogy to hadith terminology: a definition without proof is like a khabar al-wahid (a single narrator report), which cannot produce certain knowledge. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about definitions
Find in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya states that logicians say definitions cannot be prevented or proven, only refuted by naqd (counterexample) and mu'arada (counter-definition).
Some logicians restrict refutation to naqd alone.
2
Extract the refutation methods allowed
Note that for syllogisms, logicians allow muman'ah (prevention) and mu'arada, but for definitions they are more restrictive. This inconsistency is part of the critique.
3
Recognize the analogy to khabar al-wahid
Ibn Taymiyya compares the definer's unsupported statement to a single narrator report, which is susceptible to error and does not yield certainty.
In hadith studies, khabar al-wahid requires corroboration to reach the level of tawatur for certain knowledge.
4
Understand the epistemological requirement
The key point: without evidence (dalil), the listener cannot know whether the definition is correct because the definer may be mistaken or lying.
Knowledge requires a reliable source, not just a single authority.
5
Apply the critique to Islamic sciences
Consider how definitions in fiqh, usul, or tafsir must be supported by evidence from revelation or reason, not just the word of a single scholar.
This is a tool to evaluate the epistemic strength of any definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : خبر الواحد بلا دليل لا يفيد العلم الثاني : أنهم يقولون الحدّ لا يمنع ولا يقوم عليه دليل، وإنما يمكن إبطاله بـ ( النقض) و(المعارضة)، ومنهم من يقول: إنما يمكن إبطاله بالنقض فقط دون المعارضة بخلاف القياس)، فإنه يمكن فيه (الممانعة) و(المعارضة)) . فيقال : إذا لم يكن الحاد قد أقام دليلاً على صحة الحد امتنع أن يعرف المستمع المحدودَ ،به، إذا جَوّز عليه الخطأ، فإنه إذا لم يعرف صحة الحدّ إلا بقوله، وقوله محتمل للصدق والكذب، امتنع أن يعرفه بقوله
The second aspect: A single report without evidence does not benefit knowledge. The second [point]: They say that the definition cannot be prevented nor can evidence be established for it, and it can only be refuted by 'naqd' and 'mu'arada'; and among them are those who say it can only be refuted by naqd alone without mu'arada, unlike the syllogism, for which muman'ah and mu'arada are possible. So it is said: If the definer has not established evidence for the correctness of the definition, it is impossible for the listener to know the defined by it, if error is possible regarding him; because if he does not know the correctness of the definition except by his statement, and his statement is susceptible to truth and falsehood, it is impossible to know it by his statement.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is the second aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logicians' claims about definitions. He states that logicians admit that definitions cannot be proven or prevented; they can only be refuted by counterexample (naqd) or counter-definition (mu'arada). Some say only naqd applies, unlike in syllogisms where muman'ah (prevention) and mu'arada are possible. Ibn Taymiyya then argues that if the definer does not provide evidence (dalil) for his definition, the hearer cannot know whether the definition is correct, because the definer may be mistaken or lying. Since the definition is based solely on the definer's authority (a single report, khabar al-wahid), it cannot produce certain knowledge. This is a direct attack on the logicians' method of defining terms without proof.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد
النقض
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya compare a definition without evidence to 'khabar al-wahid'?
A) Because both rely on a single source that could be wrong.
B) Because both require multiple witnesses to be accepted.
C) Because both are types of analogical reasoning.
D) Because both are used in Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
A definition unsupported by evidence is epistemically weak and cannot produce certain knowledge, just like a single narrator report (khabar al-wahid).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a brief argument in your own words: Why is a definition from a single scholar without evidence not sufficient for certain knowledge? Use Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning from this passage.
?
Imagine a mufassir (Qur'anic exegete) defines 'al-Samad' based solely on his personal opinion. How would Ibn Taymiyya critique this definition using the khabar al-wahid analogy?

Ibn Taymiyya Critiques the Two-Premise and Two-Term Requirements as Arbitrary Conventions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the requirement of exactly two premises in demonstration is arbitrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique that the logicians' requirements for demonstration (two premises) and definition (genus and differentia) are arbitrary conventions, and evaluate his argument that true sciences do not depend on such conventions.

Core Idea
The logicians' rules for demonstration and definition are mere conventions, not essential truths.

After critiquing the logicians' definitional requirements, Ibn Taymiyya turns to their claim that demonstration (burhan) must consist of exactly two premises. He argues that this is as arbitrary as their rule for definition. In reality, demonstration may require one premise — which they call 'qiyas al-damir' — or more than two, which they rearrange as interlocking syllogisms. Similarly, definition does not require separate genus and differentia; one term can convey the essential attributes through implication. These rules, he concludes, are mere conventions (wad') that logicians impose without rational justification, much like someone who arbitrarily assigns different statuses to two identical persons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims being critiqued
Find in the Arabic where the logicians state that demonstration must have exactly two premises (مقدمتين) and definition must have exactly two terms (جنس وفصل). These are the targets of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's counterexamples
Note his examples: if demonstration suffices with one premise, the logicians call it 'qiyas al-damir' (syllogism of the conscience) and claim the other premise is deleted. If it requires many premises, they call them 'interlocking syllogisms'. This shows the arbitrariness.
The same flexibility applies to definition: one term can indicate the essential attributes through implication (التزام) or concomitance (تضمن).
3
Understand his conclusion about convention
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the number of premises depends on the arguer's need and prior knowledge, not on a fixed rule. Similarly, definition can be conveyed without separate genus and differentia. Therefore, the logicians' rules are mere conventions, not rational necessities.
He emphasizes that true sciences (العلوم الحقيقية) do not change with differing conventions.
4
Analyze his analogy
Read his analogy: the logicians are like someone who makes two identical persons believer/unbeliever or scholar/ignorant based on his own arbitrary stipulation, with no difference in their essences. This highlights the absurdity of imposing such conventions as if they were essential realities.
This analogy reinforces his charge that the logicians are guilty of 'differentiating between similars and equalizing between different things'.
5
Evaluate the critique's strength
Ask: does Ibn Taymiyya successfully show that these rules are arbitrary? Consider whether the logicians could respond that their conventions are not arbitrary but based on the nature of demonstration. Assess the soundness of his analogy.
The critique does not reject syllogistic reasoning itself, but only the rigid formal constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونظير هذا دعواهم أن «البرهان» لا بد أن يكون مؤلفاً من «مقدمتين» لا أقل ولا أكثر. فإن اكتفى فيه بـ «مقدّمة واحدة قالوا : الأخرى محذوفة، وسموه «قياس الضمير. وإن احتيج فيه إلى مقدّمات» قالوا: هذه «قياسات متداخلة» . فاصطلحوا على أنه لا بد في الحدّ من لفظين: «جنس» و «فصل»، ولا بدّ في «القياس» من «مقدمتين». وكل هذا تحكُم . فإن «البرهان قد يكتفي فيه بـ «مقدمة»، وقد لا يتم إلا بـ «مقدمتين»، وقد لا يتم إلا بثلاث مقدّمات وأربع وخمس، بحسب حاجة المستدلّ وما يعلمه مما لا يعلمه من «المقدّمات». وكذلك اكتفاؤهم في «الحدّ بلفظين : لفظ يدلّ على المشترك، ولفظ يدلّ على المميز، وزعمهم أن الحدّ التام لا يحصل إلا بهذا لا يحتاج إلى زيادة عليه ولا يحصل بدونه. فإنه يقال : إن أريد بـ الحد التام ما يصوّر الصفات الذاتية على التفصيل - مشتركها ومميّزها - فَـ الجنس القريب» مع «الفضل» لا يحصل ذلك . وإن أريد بما يدلّ على «الذاتيات» ولو بـ «التضمن» أو «الالتزام) فَـ «الفصل») بل «الخاصة» يدل على ذلك . وإذا عارضهم مَنْ يوجب ذكر جميع «الأجناس» أو يحذف جميعها لم يكن لهم عنه جواب إلا أن هذا وضعهم واصطلاحهم. ومعلوم أن العلوم الحقيقية لا تختلف باختلاف الأوضاع فقد تبين أن ما ذكروه هو من باب الوضع والاصطلاح الذي جعلوه من باب الحقائق الذاتية والمعارف. وهذا عين الضلال والإضلال كمن ء إلى شخصين متماثلين يجعل هذا مؤمناً وهذا كافراً، وهذا عالماً وهذا جاهلاً، يجيء . وهذا سعيداً وهذا شقياً، من غير افتراق بين ذاتيهما ،وصفاتهما، بل بمجرد وضعه واصطلاحه. فهم مع دعواهم القياس العقليّ يفرقون بين المتماثلات ويسوون بين المختلفات
Similar to this is their claim that demonstration must be composed of exactly two premises, no fewer and no more. If it suffices with one premise, they say: the other is deleted, and they call it 'syllogism of the conscience'. If it requires multiple premises, they say: these are 'interlocking syllogisms'. They term that in definition there must be two terms: 'genus' and 'differentia', and in syllogism there must be two premises. All this is arbitrary. For demonstration may suffice with one premise, may not be completed except with two premises, may not be completed except with three, four, five premises, according to the need of the arguer and what he knows and does not know of the premises. Similarly, their contentment in definition with two words: a word indicating the common, and a word indicating the distinctive, and their claim that the complete definition is only attained by this, does not need addition, and cannot be without it. For it is said: If by complete definition is meant what depicts the essential attributes in detail—shared and distinctive—then the proximate genus with the differentia does not achieve that. And if what is meant is what indicates the essential attributes even by 'concomitance' or 'implication', then the differentia, or even the property (khassa), indicates that. And if someone opposes them by requiring mentioning all genera or deleting all, they have no answer except that this is their convention and convention. It is known that true sciences do not differ with differing conventions. So it has become clear that what they mentioned is from the chapter of convention and terminology which they made from the chapter of essential realities and cognitions. This is pure misguidance and misleading, like someone who comes to two identical persons and makes this one a believer and that one an unbeliever, this one a scholar and that one an ignorant, this one happy and that one wretched, without any difference in their essences and attributes, but merely by his own convention and terminology. So they, with their claim of rational syllogism, differentiate between similars and equalize between different things.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage extends the critique from definition to demonstration (burhan). Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' insistence on exactly two premises in a syllogism is as arbitrary as their rule for definition. In reality, demonstration may require one premise, two, three, or more depending on the context and prior knowledge of the arguer. Similarly, in definition, requiring exactly two terms (genus and differentia) is not necessary because the same information can be conveyed by a single term through implication or by listing all attributes. The logicians provide no rational justification for these rules; they are mere conventions (wad') that they have imposed. Ibn Taymiyya compares their arbitrary distinctions to someone who makes two identical persons have opposite statuses (believer/unbeliever) based solely on his own convention, which is clearly absurd. Therefore, the logicians' claim to be following rational, necessary truths is false; they are actually imposing arbitrary terminological conventions.

Key Terms
البرهان
قياس الضمير
المقدمات
الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya say about the required number of premises in a demonstration?
A) It must always be exactly two.
B) It can vary depending on the need.
C) It must be at least three.
D) It must be exactly one.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' rules for demonstration and definition are arbitrary conventions, not essential rational necessities, and therefore true knowledge is not bound by them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example in Islamic jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh) where a ruling is derived using more than two premises (e.g., qiyas with multiple analogical similarities). Explain why the number of premises is not fixed and how Ibn Taymiyya's critique would apply.
?
In a debate, someone argues that a valid demonstration must have exactly two premises. Using Ibn Taymiyya's argument, show how this is an arbitrary convention, not a universal requirement.

Critique of the Two-Premise Requirement in Burhan
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the requirement of exactly two premises in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the logicians' requirement of exactly two premises in a demonstration is an arbitrary convention, not a logical necessity.

Core Idea
Demonstrations can have one, two, or many premises; the logicians' rule is mere convention.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: that a valid syllogism must have exactly two premises. He points out that a demonstration (burhan) may need only one premise, or three, four, or more, depending on the arguer's needs. In this lesson, you will analyze his critique and understand why he labels the logicians' rule as 'tahakkum' (arbitrary judgment).

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
What do the logicians assert about the number of premises in a burhan? They claim it must be composed of exactly two premises, no less and no more.
Look for the phrase 'مؤلفاً من مقدمتين'.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's counterexamples
How does he show that a demonstration can have one premise (qiyas al-damir) or multiple premises (interlocking syllogisms)? He gives examples of implicit syllogisms and notes that the number varies by need.
His examples show the logicians' own reclassifications.
3
Analyze his charge of arbitrariness
Why does he call this rule 'tahakkum' and compare it to arbitrary classification? He argues that the logicians have no objective basis; they merely stipulate conventions.
The analogy with arbitrary believer/disbeliever classification highlights the problem.
4
Examine the broader implication
What does this reveal about the status of logical rules according to Ibn Taymiyya? They are not essential truths but human conventions that can differ.
He concludes that real sciences do not change with conventions.

ونظير هذا دعواهم أن «البرهان» لا بد أن يكون مؤلفاً من «مقدمتين» لا أقل ولا أكثر. فإن اكتفى فيه بـ «مقدّمة واحدة» قالوا: الأخرى محذوفة، وسموه «قياس الضمير». وإن احتيج فيه إلى «مقدّمات» قالوا: هذه «قياسات متداخلة». فاصطلحوا على أنه لا بد في الحدّ من لفظين: «جنس» و «فصل»، ولا بدّ في «القياس» من «مقدمتين». وكل هذا تحكّم. فإن «البرهان» قد يكتفي فيه بـ «مقدمة»، وقد لا يتم إلا بـ «مقدمتين»، وقد لا يتم إلا بثلاث مقدّمات وأربع وخمس، بحسب حاجة المستدلّ وما يعلمه مما لا يعلمه من «المقدّمات». وكذلك اكتفاؤهم في «الحدّ» بلفظين: لفظ يدلّ على المشترك، ولفظ يدلّ على المميز، وزعمهم أن الحدّ التام لا يحصل إلا بهذا لا يحتاج إلى زيادة عليه ولا يحصل بدونه. فإنه يقال: إن أريد بـ «الحدّ التام» ما يصوّر الصفات الذاتية على التفصيل - مشتركها ومميّزها - فالجنس القريب مع الفصل لا يحصل ذلك. وإن أريد بما يدلّ على «الذاتيات» ولو بـ «التضمّن» أو «الالتزام» فالفصل بل الخاصة يدلّ على ذلك. وإذا عارضهم من يوجب ذكر جميع «الأجناس» أو يحذف والاصطلاح لم يكن لهم عنه جواب إلا أن هذا وضعهم واصطلاحهم. ومعلوم أن العلوم الحقيقية لا تختلف باختلاف الأوضاع. فقد تبين أن ما ذكروه هو من باب الوضع والاصطلاح الذي جعلوه من باب الحقائق الذاتية والمعارف. وهذا عين الضلال والإضلال كمن يأتي إلى شخصين متماثلين يجعل هذا مؤمناً وهذا كافراً، وهذا عالماً وهذا جاهلاً، وهذا سعيداً وهذا شقياً، من غير افتراق بين ذاتيهما وصفاتهما، بل بمجرد وضعه واصطلاحه. فهم مع دعواهم القياس العقليّ يفرقون بين المتماثلات ويسوّون بين المختلفات.
And similar to this is their claim that the demonstration must be composed of two premises, no less and no more. If one suffices with one premise, they say: the other is omitted, and they call it 'qiyas al-damir' (implicit syllogism). And if more than two premises are needed, they say: these are 'interlocking syllogisms'. So they stipulated that in a definition there must be two terms: genus and differentia, and in a syllogism there must be two premises. All of this is arbitrary judgment. For a demonstration may suffice with one premise, may not be completed except with two premises, and may not be completed except with three, four, or five premises, according to the need of the arguer and what he knows and does not know of the premises. Similarly, their sufficing with two terms in definition: a term indicating the common (genus) and a term indicating the distinguishing (differentia), and their claim that the perfect definition is only attained by these, needing no addition and not attained without them. For it is said: if by 'perfect definition' is meant that which depicts the essential attributes in detail—their common and distinguishing—then the proximate genus with the differentia does not achieve that. And if what is meant is that which indicates the essential attributes even by inclusion or implication, then the differentia, or even the property (khassa), indicates that. And if someone opposes them, requiring the mention of all the genera or omitting them, and [makes] the stipulation, they have no answer except that this is their convention and stipulation. And it is known that real sciences do not differ with differences of conventions. So it has become clear that what they mentioned belongs to the category of conventional stipulation, which they have made into essential truths and knowledge. And this is pure misguidance and misdirection, like someone who takes two identical persons and makes one a believer and the other a disbeliever, one knowledgeable and one ignorant, one happy and one wretched, without any difference between their essences and attributes, but merely by his own convention and stipulation. So they, with their claim of rational syllogism, differentiate between identicals and equate between different things.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a critique of logicians' claims regarding the necessary structure of demonstration (burhan) and definition (hadd). Ibn Taymiyya begins by analogizing: just as logicians insist that a demonstration must have exactly two premises, and if only one is given they call it 'qiyas al-damir' (implicit syllogism), or if more they call them interlocking syllogisms, similarly they insist that a definition must have exactly two terms: the genus (jins) and the differentia (fasl). Ibn Taymiyya calls this 'tahakkum' (arbitrary judgment). He argues that a demonstration may need one, two, three, or more premises depending on the situation. Likewise, regarding the definition, if the aim is to fully depict all essential attributes, then even the proximate genus and differentia are insufficient. If the aim is merely to indicate the essentials by any means (inclusion or implication), then even the differentia or the property (khassa) suffices. He then points out that if someone objects by requiring all the genera or omitting them, the logicians have no answer except to say 'this is our convention.' Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that real sciences do not change with conventions. He likens this to someone arbitrarily declaring two identical persons to have opposite statuses (believer/disbeliever, etc.) based solely on his own convention. Thus, the logicians, despite claiming rational reasoning, end up differentiating between identicals and equating between different things.

قياس الضمير
قياسات متداخلة
تحكم

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the basis of the logicians' requirement that a burhan must have exactly two premises?
A) Empirical observation
B) Divine revelation
C) Arbitrary convention (wad' and istilah)
D) Inductive generalization from many demonstrations

Key Takeaway
The number of premises in a demonstration is not fixed; it depends on the context, and logicians' insistence on two premises is an arbitrary convention.

?
Construct a demonstration with only one premise on an Islamic topic (e.g., 'The Quran is the word of God because Muhammad was illiterate and could not have composed it') and explain why it still counts as a demonstration despite having only one premise.

Critique of the Two-Term Requirement in Hadd
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the requirement of exactly two terms (genus and differentia) in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that a perfect definition does not require exactly two terms (genus and differentia) and that other forms of indication suffice.

Core Idea
Definitions can use more than two terms, or even single terms via inclusion or implication.

Just as logicians insisted on two premises in syllogisms, they also insisted that a definition (hadd) must consist of exactly two terms: genus and differentia. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is also arbitrary. He points out that a perfect definition aiming to depict all essential attributes may need more than two terms, and even a single term can indicate the essence through inclusion (tadamun) or implication (iltizam).

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' position
What do they claim about the composition of a hadd? They claim it must consist of exactly two terms: a term indicating the common (genus) and a term indicating the distinguishing (differentia).
Look for 'لا بد في الحدّ من لفظين: جنس وفصل'.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's distinction
He distinguishes between two aims: depicting all essential attributes in detail versus merely indicating the essence. Which aim are the logicians pursuing?
The passage discusses 'تصوير الصفات الذاتية' vs 'الدلالة على الذاتيات'.
3
Analyze the alternative modes of indication
How can a single term (like the differentia or even the property) indicate the essence via inclusion (tadamun) or implication (iltizam)?
For example, 'house' includes 'wall' (tadamun) and implies 'ceiling' (iltizam).
4
Appreciate the charge of arbitrariness
If someone objects and demands all genera, logicians have no answer except 'this is our convention'. Why is this problematic for their claim of objective logic?
Ibn Taymiyya stresses that real sciences do not change with conventions.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Restricting Universal Proofs
Author refutes logicians' restriction on universal proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of knowledge (mutawātir, tajriba, ḥads) that logicians claim are private, and explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that they can be universal proofs.

Core Idea
Knowledge gained by sense, experience, or testimony can be shared as proof; no type is inherently private.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya addresses the claim by some logicians that certain kinds of knowledge — those based on repeated testimony (tawātur), experiment (tajriba), or intuition (ḥads) — cannot serve as universal evidence in argumentation. He argues that this distinction is corrupt, using the example of moon sighting to show how sensory knowledge can be communicated to others. This lesson will walk through his critique and the epistemological assumptions behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find the passage where Ibn Taymiyya states that some logicians say propositions known through tawātur, tajriba, and ḥads are exclusive to those who know them and thus not a proof against others.
This claim is the target of the critique.
2
Examine the moon sighting example
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of a person seeing the crescent moon and stating 'the crescent has risen.' For the observer it is sensory; for others it may be doubtful or false. Yet the observer can still use evidence to prove it to others.
This shows that sensory knowledge is not inherently private.
3
Trace the argument structure
Ibn Taymiyya argues that people differ in their need for evidence, but that does not make some types of knowledge inherently non-universal. He shows that even sensory knowledge can be communicated through examples, rhetorical questions, and middle terms tailored to the audience.
The key is that knowledge can be transferred; the method of knowing does not dictate its communicability.
4
Assess the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya declares the logicians' distinction 'corrupt' (fāsid). Consider why: if sensory knowledge can be made common proof, then tawātur, tajriba, and ḥads can also be communicated through testimony, reasoning, or analogies.
This critique undermines a foundational epistemological claim of the logicians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الناس ليسوا سواء في احتياجهم إلى «دليل» فإن كثيرا من الناس تكون القضية عنده «حِسّية» أو «مُجرَّبة» أو «مبرهنة» أو «متواترة»، وغيره إنما عَرَفها بالنظر والاستدلال. ولهذا كثير من الناس لا يحتاج في ثبوت المحمول» لـ «الموضوع» إلى «دليل» لنفسه، بل لغيره. ويبين ذلك لغيره بأدلة هو غني عنها، حتى يضرب له أمثالاً، ويقول له «أليس كذا؟ أليس كذا؟»، ويحتج عليه من الأدلة العقلية والسمعية بما يكون «حداً أوسط» عند المخاطب مما لا يحتاج إليه المستدِلُّ. بل قد يعلم الشيء بـ «الحسن» ويستدل على ثبوته لغيره بـ «الدليل». وهذا أكبر من أن يحتاج إلى تمثيل فما أكثر من يرى الكواكب ويرى الهلال وغيره، فيقول «قد طلع الهلال». وتكون هذه القضية له حسية»، وقد تكون عند غيره مشكوكاً فيها، أو مظنونة أو خبرية، بل قد يظنها كذباً إذا صَدَّق المنجِّمَ الخارص القائل: «إنه لا يُرَى (۱) ) . وقد ذَكَر مَن ذَكَر مِن هؤلاء المنطقيين أن القضايا المعلومة بـ «التواتر» و «التجربة» و «الحَدْس» يختص بها مَنْ علمها بهذا الطريق فلا تكون حجة على غيره ؛ بخلاف غيرها، فإنها مشتركة يُحتج بها على المنازع. [۷۹] وقد بينا في غير هذا الموضع أن هذا تفريق فاسد.
The third section — People are not equal in their need for ‘evidence’ (dalīl). For many people, a proposition is ‘sensory’ (ḥissiyya) or ‘experiential’ (mujarriba) or ‘demonstrated’ (mubarhana) or ‘mutawātir’ (mutawātira), while another knows it only through reflection and inference. For this reason, many people do not need ‘evidence’ for the affirmation of the ‘predicate’ for the ‘subject’ for themselves, but for others. And they explain it to others with evidence they themselves are independent of, to the point that they give examples and say to him: ‘Is it not such? Is it not such?’ and argue against him with intellectual and transmitted evidence that serves as a ‘middle term’ (ḥadd awsaṭ) for the interlocutor, which the proponent does not need. Rather, one may know a thing through the ‘senses’ and then use ‘evidence’ to prove its existence to others. This is too obvious to need illustration: how many people see the stars and see the crescent moon and say, ‘The crescent has risen.’ This proposition is ‘sensory’ for him, but for another it may be doubtful, or conjectural, or hearsay; indeed, he may suspect it is false if he trusts the astrologer-foreteller who says, ‘It will not be seen (1).’ And some of these logicians have mentioned that propositions known through ‘tawātur’ (concurrent testimony), ‘experiment’ (tajriba), and ‘intuition’ (ḥads) are exclusive to those who know them by that method, so they are not a proof against others; unlike others [propositions], which are common and can be used as proof against an opponent. [79] And we have shown elsewhere that this distinction is corrupt.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' claim that certain types of knowledge cannot be used as universal proofs. The author begins by stating the third topic: people differ in their need for evidence. He explains that a proposition may be sensory (like seeing the crescent moon), experiential (like knowing that fire burns through repeated observation), demonstrated (through a proof), or mutawātir (transmitted by many). Yet another person may only know it through reasoning. Consequently, a person may not need evidence for himself but may use it for others, employing examples and rhetorical questions like 'Is it not such?' He uses the example of the moon sighting: someone sees the crescent and says it has risen, but another may doubt it or consider it false if an astrologer claimed it would not be seen. This shows how sensory knowledge varies. The author then directly refutes the logicians who claim that propositions known by tawātur, tajriba, and ḥads are exclusive to the knower and cannot be used as proof against others. He declares this distinction corrupt. In Islamic law, for example, the testimony of a single reliable witness (khabar wāḥid) is not binding like mutawātir, but the author argues that the logicians' restriction is too narrow: even sensory knowledge can be communicated and become a common proof. The passage emphasizes that the nature of evidence varies, but that does not mean some proofs are inherently non-universal.

مجربة
مبرهنة
متواترة
حدس
دليل
حد أوسط
تواتر
تجربة

According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the logicians' claim that propositions known by tawātur are not universal proofs corrupt?
A) Because tawātur is always more certain than demonstration.
B) Because knowledge via tawātur can be communicated and become a common proof, just like sensory knowledge.
C) Because only sensory propositions are universal.
D) Because logicians never actually studied tawātur.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that knowledge types are not inherently private; any known proposition can be communicated and used as evidence with appropriate reasoning.

?

Demonstration -- Burhan

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Universal Premises in Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the necessity of universal premises in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the requirement of universal premises in demonstration leads to an epistemic vicious circle or infinite regress, and evaluate its implications for theological epistemology.

Core Idea
Requiring universal premises for all demonstration creates an infinite regress or circularity that cannot be avoided.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: the necessity of a universal affirmative premise in demonstrative syllogisms (burhan). He argues that if every demonstration requires a universal premise, then knowledge of that premise itself must be justified, leading to a dilemma: either the universal premise is intuitive (making the universal unnecessary) or it is theoretical, which requires further justification ad infinitum or in a circle. He extends this to all types of premises the logicians consider foundational, exposing a fundamental epistemic flaw in their system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that theoretical certain knowledge (al-‘ulum al-yaqiniyya al-nazariyya) is only obtained through demonstration, which requires a universal affirmative premise. They also claim that two negative or two particular premises never yield a conclusion in any syllogistic form (categorical, conditional, disjunctive) or material type (demonstrative, rhetorical, dialectical, poetic).
This claim is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's first objection
He asks: To know that a premise is universal, you must have knowledge of its universality. If the premise could be particular or indefinite (which is equivalent to particular), then certain knowledge does not result. So you must be certain that it is universal.
This sets up the epistemological problem.
3
Analyze the dilemma
The universal premise is either intuitive (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). If intuitive, then each of its individual instances could also be intuitive a fortiori, making the universal premise redundant. If theoretical, then it requires justification from an intuitive premise, creating a regress or circle.
Ibn Taymiyya uses the term 'dawr ma'i' (vicious circle) and 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) for the second horn.
4
Recognize the vicious circle and regress
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if theoretical knowledge depends on demonstration, and demonstration depends on universal premises, which are themselves theoretical, then you have a circle. Alternatively, you get an infinite regress of justifications. He notes that this regress is unlike the regress of past events because it is a regress in efficient causes, and it is impossible because human knowledge has a beginning.
He explicitly rejects both circle and regress as false.
5
Extend to all foundational premises
Ibn Taymiyya generalizes the critique to all types of premises the logicians accept as foundational: sensory (external and internal), experiential (mujarrabat), mutawatir, and intuitive (hadsiyyat). He implies that none can serve as certain universal premises without facing the same dilemma.
This shows the breadth of his critique.

بطلان دعواهم : لا بد في «البرهان» من «قضية كلية» . وأيضاً، فإذا قالوا : العلوم اليقينية النظرية لا تحصل إلا بـ «البرهان»، الذي عندهم قياس شمولي». وعندهم لا بد فيه من «قضية كلية موجبة» . ولهذا قالوا إنه لا نتاج عن قضيتين سالبتين) ولا جزئيتين» في شيء من أنواع القياس، لا بحسب صورته ، كـ «الحَمْلي»، و «الشرطي المتصل»، و «المنفصل»؛ ولا مادته، لا «البرهاني»، ولا «الخَطَابي»، ولا «الجدلي»، بل ولا بحسب «الشعري». فيقال : إذا كان لا بد في كل ما يسمونه «برهاناً» من «قضية كلية فلا بد من العلم العلم يكون بتلك القضية الكلية، أي من العلم بكونها «كلية» . وإلا ، فمتى جُوّز عليها أن لا تكون كلية) بل جزئية لم يحصل العلم بموجبها . و «المهملة» ـ وهي المطلقة التي يحتمل لفظها أن تكون كلية ) و (جزئية» - في قوة «الجزئية» . وإذا كان لا بد في العلم الحاصل بالقياس - الذي يخصونه باسم «البرهان» - من العلم بـ «قضية كلية موجبة»، فيقال: العلم بتلك القضية إن كان بديهياً أمكن أن يكون كلُّ واحد من أفرادها «بديهيا» بطريق الأولى. وإن كان نظرياً» احتاج إلى علم بديهي فيفضي إلى الدور المَعِي» أو التسلسل في أمور لها مبدأ محدود». فإن علم ابن آدم إذا توقف على علم منه، وعلمه على علم منه ، فعلمه له مبدأ، لأنه نفسه له مبدأ ؛ ليس هذا كَـ تسلسل الحوادث الماضية ؛ وأيضاً فإنه تسلسل في «المؤثرات». وكلاهما باطل . وهكذا يقال في سائر القضايا الكلية التي يجعلونها مبادىء البرهان» ويسمونها الواجبُ قبولها». سواء كانت حسية» ظاهرة أو باطنة، وهي التي يحسها بنفسه ; أو من المجربات» أو «المتواترات» أو «الحدسيات» - عند من يجعل منها ما هو من اليقينيات الواجب «قبولها.
Refutation of their claim: In demonstration, a universal proposition is necessary. Also, if they say: Theoretical certain sciences are not obtained except through 'demonstration', which according to them is a universal syllogism. And according to them, a universal affirmative proposition is necessary in it. Hence they said that nothing results from two negative propositions nor from two particular propositions in any type of syllogism, neither in terms of its form, like the categorical, the conditional conjunctive, and the disjunctive; nor in terms of its matter, neither the demonstrative, nor the rhetorical, nor the dialectical, nor even according to the poetic. So it is said: If in everything they call 'demonstration' a universal proposition is necessary, then knowledge of that universal proposition is necessary, i.e., knowledge that it is universal. Otherwise, if it is possible that it is not universal but particular, then certain knowledge by it is not obtained. And the 'indefinite' — which is the absolute whose expression can be universal or particular — is in force of the particular. And if in the knowledge obtained by syllogism — which they specifically call 'demonstration' — knowledge of a universal affirmative proposition is necessary, then it is said: If that proposition is intuitive, then it would be possible for each of its individuals to be intuitive a fortiori. And if it is theoretical, it needs intuitive knowledge, leading to vicious circle or infinite regress in matters that have a limited beginning. For the knowledge of the son of Adam, if it depends on knowledge from it, and his knowledge depends on knowledge from it, then his knowledge has a beginning, because he himself has a beginning; this is not like the regress of past events; also it is a regress in 'efficient causes'. And both are false. And similarly it is said regarding all universal propositions which they make as premises of demonstration and call them 'necessary to accept', whether they are sensory, apparent or internal, which one senses himself, or from experiential, or from mutawatir, or from intuitive — according to those who consider some of them to be certainties necessary to accept.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that demonstration (burhan) necessarily requires a universal premise. Ibn Taymiyya first states the logicians' position: theoretical certain sciences are only obtained through a universal affirmative premise, and they claim that no conclusion can be derived from two negatives or two particulars in any form of syllogism (categorical, conditional conjunctive, disjunctive) or material type (demonstrative, rhetorical, dialectical, poetic). He then begins his critique: if a universal premise is necessary, one must know that it is indeed universal. If it is not known to be universal, it becomes particular or indefinite (which is equivalent to particular). But if the universal premise itself is known, it must be either intuitive (badihi) or theoretical. If it is intuitive, then each of its individual instances could be intuitive a fortiori — so why need the universal? If it is theoretical, it requires a prior intuitive premise, leading either to a vicious circle (dawr ma'i) or an infinite regress (tasalsul) in matters that have a beginning, which is impossible. He distinguishes this from the regress of past events; this is a regress in efficient causes, both of which are false. He then extends the argument to all types of premises (sensory, experiential, mutawatir, intuitive) that they call 'necessary to accept', implying they all suffer from the same problem.

A) It must be accepted on faith without proof.

D) It can be known directly through sensory experience.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the logicians' requirement of universal premises in all demonstration leads to an epistemic dilemma: either the universality is intuitive (making the universal superfluous) or theoretical (leading to an impossible circle or regress).

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
8 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Necessity of Universal Premises
Ibn Taymiyya argues against the logicians' claimed necessity of universal premises for certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the logicians' claim that certain knowledge requires universal premises, and evaluate its internal validity.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge of particulars does not require universal premises – direct apprehension suffices.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya directly challenges the foundational claim of Aristotelian logic that certain knowledge (yaqīn) must be derived from universal premises (qaḍāyā kulliyya) via demonstration (burhān). He argues that we know many particulars directly, without needing the universal. This lesson will walk you through his argument, its examples, and its logical structure, and then critically assess it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
Find the thesis that Ibn Taymiyya opposes: that certain sciences (al-ʿulūm al-yaqīniyya) necessarily depend on universal propositions (al-qaḍāyā al-kulliyya) and the syllogism they call burhān.
This is stated in the opening sentence.
2
List the types of universal premises mentioned
Ibn Taymiyya enumerates the kinds of premises the logicians consider necessary: sensory (ḥissiyya), experiential (mujarrabāt), widespread reports (tawātur), and intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt). Note that he disputes whether intuition yields certainty.
These are the 'principles of demonstration' (mabādiʾ al-burhān).
3
Analyze his examples of knowledge without universal premises
He gives examples: knowing that this particular thing is half of two, that this whole is greater than its part, that this thing is not both black and white, that a particular thing cannot be both existent and non-existent. For each, he argues the particular is known directly, not via the universal.
The universal premise is not needed as a middle term.
4
Examine his logical analysis of the syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya points out that the universal proposition gives the major term (al-ḥadd al-akbar), but without the minor premise (al-ṣughrā) you cannot conclude about the particular. Yet the conclusion about the particular is often known even without the major premise. Therefore, the syllogism is not necessary.
He uses the example of opposites and contradictories to illustrate this point.
5
Assess his conclusion about the term 'burhān'
He concludes that the logicians' restricted definition of burhān is not the Islamic usage. In the Qur'an and scholarly tradition, burhān means any clear proof, not just a syllogism with certain premises.
This is a linguistic and epistemic argument that broadens the concept of demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - العلوم اليقينية لا تفتقر بالضرورة إلى القضايا الكلية القضايا «الحسية» وهكذا يقال في سائر القضايا الكلية التي يجعلونها مبادىء البرهان ويسمونها الواجبُ قبولها سواء كانت حسية ظاهرة أو باطنة وهي التي يحسها بنفسه أو من المجربات أو المتواترات أو الحَدسيات عند من يجعل منها ما هو من اليقينيات الواجب قبولها مثل العلم بكون ضوء القمر مستفاداً من الشمس إذا رأى اختلاف أشكاله عند اختلاف محاذياته للشمس كما يختلف إذا فارقها بعد الاجتماع كما في ليلة الهلال وإذا كان ليلة الاستقبال عند الأبدار وهم متنازعون هل الحدس قد يفيد اليقين أم لا ومثل العقليات المحضة كقولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين والكل ذلك البرهان بل هو الواقع كثيراً فإذا علم أن كل واحد فهو نصف كل اثنين وأن كل اثنين نصفهم واحد فإنه يعلم أن هذا الواحد نصف هذين الاثنين وهلم جرا في سائر القضايا المعينة من غير استدلال على ذلك بالقضية الكلية وكذلك كل كل وجزء يمكن العلم بأن هذا الكل أعظم من جزئه بدون توسط القضية الكلية وكذلك هذان النقيضان من تصورهما نقيضين فإنه يعلم أنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان فكل أحد يعلم أن هذا المعين لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً ولا يخلو من الوجود والعدم كما يعلم المعين الآخر ولا يحتاج ذلك إلى أن يستدل عليه بأن كل شيء لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً معاً وكذلك الضدان فإن الإنسان يعلم أن هذا الشيء لا يكون أسود أبيض ولا يجتمعان وإن لم يعلم تضادهما لم يغنه العلم بالقضية الكلية وهي علمه بأن كل ضدين لا يجتمعان فإن العلم بالقضية الكلية يفيد العلم بالمقدمة الكبرى المشتملة على الحد الأكبر وذلك لا يغني بدون العلم بالمقدمة الصغرى المشتملة على الحد الأصغر والعلم بالنتيجة وهو أن هذين المعينين ضدان فلا يجتمعان يمكن بدون العلم بالمقدمة الكبرى وهو أن كل ضدين لا يجتمعان فلم يفتقر العلم بذلك إلى القياس الذي خصوه باسم البرهان The third station - the certain sciences do not necessarily depend on universal propositions. The sensory propositions, and likewise all universal propositions that they make principles of demonstration and name 'the obligatory to accept', whether they are apparent or internal sensory, which he senses himself, or from experiential, or mutawatir, or intuitive - according to those who consider some of them as certainties obligatory to accept - such as knowledge that the light of the moon is derived from the sun when one sees the variation of its phases at different alignments to the sun, as it varies when it separates from it after conjunction like on the night of the crescent, and when it is the night of opposition at the full moons; and they dispute whether intuition may yield certainty or not. And like pure rational propositions, such as our saying: 'the one is half of two', 'the whole is greater than the part', 'things equal to the same thing are equal', 'two opposites do not coexist', 'two contradictories neither coexist nor both vanish'. So there is no proposition among these universal propositions that is made a premise in demonstration except that knowledge of the conclusion is possible without the mediation of that demonstration; indeed, that often occurs. For if one knows that every one is half of two, and that every two are half of one, then he knows that this one is half of these two; and so on for all particular propositions without inference from the universal proposition. Likewise, every whole and part: one can know that this whole is greater than its part without the mediation of the universal proposition. Likewise, these two contradictories: whoever conceives them as contradictories knows that they cannot coexist and cannot both vanish; so everyone knows that this particular thing cannot be both existent and non-existent, and cannot be free of existence and non-existence, just as he knows the other particular. He does not need to infer that by [the proposition] 'every thing cannot be both existent and non-existent together'. Likewise, the two opposites: a person knows that this thing cannot be black and white, cannot be moving and stationary, just as he knows that the other is so. He does not need in that knowledge a universal proposition that 'every thing cannot be black and white' and 'cannot be moving and stationary'. Likewise for everything whose opposition is known: for if one knows the opposition of two particulars, he knows they cannot coexist; but if he does not know their opposition, the knowledge of the universal proposition—that 'every two opposites cannot coexist'—does not suffice him. For knowledge of the universal proposition yields knowledge of the major premise containing the major term, but that does not suffice without knowledge of the minor premise containing the minor term. And knowledge of the conclusion—that these two particulars are opposites and thus cannot coexist—is possible without knowledge of the major premise, which is that 'every two opposites cannot coexist'. So knowledge of that does not depend on the syllogism that they specifically call 'burhan'. However, burhan in the speech of Allah and His Messenger and in the speech of all classes of scholars is not restricted to what they call burhan. They have merely restricted the term 'burhan' to that which includes the syllogism whose form and matter they have specified as mentioned.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that certain knowledge (al-ʿulūm al-yaqīniyya) necessarily depends on universal premises (al-qaḍāyā al-kulliyya) and the syllogism they call demonstration (al-burhān). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating his thesis: certain knowledge does not necessarily require universal premises. He then lists the types of universal premises the logicians use as principles of demonstration: sensory (ḥissiyya), experiential (mujarrabāt), widespread reports (mutawāwirāt), and intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt) for those who include them as certain. He gives an example: knowledge that moonlight is derived from the sun is gained by observation, not by a universal premise. Next, he discusses pure rational propositions like 'the one is half of two' and 'the whole is greater than the part'. He argues that each particular instance of such a proposition is known directly without inferring it from the universal. For example, one knows that this white thing is not black without needing the universal 'every thing is not black and white'. He extends this to contradictories and opposites: everyone knows that a particular thing cannot both exist and not exist without reasoning from the universal. He then analyzes the logical structure: the universal premise gives the major term, but without the minor premise specifying the particular, no conclusion follows. Yet the conclusion about the particular is known even without the universal major premise. Therefore, the specific syllogism the logicians call 'burhān' is not necessary. He concludes by noting that the term 'burhān' in Islamic scripture and scholarly discourse is broader than the logicians' restricted usage. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya defends the sufficiency of direct, particular knowledge and dismisses the indispensability of formal logical demonstration.

اليقين
الحدس

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Ahwal Argument
Ibn Taymiyya applies his critique to the ahwal argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that the ahwal argument requires a universal syllogistic premise.

Core Idea
Particular knowledge does not depend on universal premises.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya applies his critique of demonstrative syllogism to a specific theological argument about 'states' (ahwal). He argues that the logical refutation of ahwal does not need the universal premise that every two contradictories cannot coexist. Instead, one knows directly that a particular state cannot be both existent and non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' argument form
The logicians use a categorical syllogism: major premise 'every two contradictories neither coexist nor both vanish', minor premise 'existence and non-existence are contradictories', conclusion 'a state cannot be both existent and non-existent'.
2
Locate the universal premise in their reasoning
The universal premise is 'every two contradictories neither coexist nor both vanish'. This is the major premise that the logicians claim is essential.
3
Notice Ibn Taymiyya's claim about particular knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya states that knowledge that this particular thing (a state) cannot be existent and non-existent is possible without that universal premise. The particular conclusion is known directly.
4
Understand why this undermines the necessity of demonstration
If the particular conclusion is known without the universal, then the syllogistic form is not needed for this knowledge. The logicians' claim of demonstrative necessity is weakened.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال ذلك أنه إذا أريد إبطال قول من يثبت الأحوال ويقول إنها لا موجودة ولا معدومة فقيل هذان نقيضان وكل نقيضين لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان فإن هذا جعل للواحد لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً ولا يمكن جعل شيء من الأشياء لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً في حال واحدة فلا يمكن جعل الحال لا موجودة ولا معدومة كان العلم بأن هذا المعين لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً ممكناً بدون هذه القضية الكلية فلا يفتقر العلم بالنتيجة إلى البرهان وكذلك إذا قيل إن هذا ممكن وكل ممكن فلا بد له من مرجح لوجوده على عدمه على أصح القولين أو الأحد طرفيه على قول طائفة من الناس أو قيل هذا محدث وكل محدث فلا بد له من محدث فتلك القضية الكلية وهي قولنا كل محدث لا بد له من محدث وكل ممكن لا بد له من مرجح يمكن العلم بأفرادها
Example of that: when one intends to refute the position of those who affirm 'states' (al-aḥwāl) and say they are neither existent nor non-existent, and it is said: 'These two are contradictories, and every two contradictories neither coexist nor both vanish'—for this makes the one [thing] neither existent nor non-existent, and it is impossible to make any thing neither existent nor non-existent in a single state, so it is impossible to make a 'state' neither existent nor non-existent—knowledge that this particular thing cannot be existent and non-existent is possible without this universal proposition, so knowledge of the conclusion does not depend on demonstration. Likewise, when it is said: 'This is possible, and every possible must have a preponderating factor for its existence over its non-existence'—according to the more correct of the two opinions, or 'a preponderator of one of its two sides' according to a group of people—or it is said: 'This is originated (ḥādith), and every originated must have an originator'—that universal proposition, namely our saying 'every originated must have an originator' and 'every possible must have a preponderator', knowledge of its individuals is possible [without it].
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage provides two concrete examples from Islamic theology (kalām) to illustrate Ibn Taymiyya's earlier claim that certain knowledge of particulars does not require universal premises or syllogistic demonstration. The first example addresses the proponents of 'states' (aḥwāl), a theological position that posits entities that are neither existent nor non-existent. The refutation typically uses a syllogism: 'These two (existent and non-existent) are contradictories; every two contradictories cannot coexist and cannot both vanish; therefore, a state cannot be simultaneously existent and non-existent.' Ibn Taymiyya argues that one already knows that this particular thing cannot be both existent and non-existent directly, without the universal premise 'every two contradictories neither coexist nor both vanish.' The universal is unnecessary because the particular knowledge is immediate. The second example involves two classic kalām arguments: the possibility argument (that a possible being needs a preponderator) and the temporal origination argument (that an originated thing needs an originator). In each case, the logicians claim that the universal premise (e.g., 'every originated must have an originator') is essential. Ibn Taymiyya counters that knowledge of the particular instance (e.g., that this world is originated and thus needs a creator) is known without the universal; one can know the particular conclusion directly. Thus, the demonstrative syllogism is not required for these theological proofs, undermining the logicians' claim of its indispensability.

Key Terms
الأحوال
النقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the particular conclusion is known directly without it
C) Because the minor premise is sufficient
D) Because the syllogism is in the second figure

Key Takeaway
The refutation of ahwal does not require a universal premise about contradictories because the particular knowledge is immediate.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Huduth and Imkan Arguments
Ibn Taymiyya applies his critique to huduth and imkan arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that the huduth and imkan arguments do not depend on universal premises.

Core Idea
The particular origination of the world is known directly, not through a syllogism.

Ibn Taymiyya extends his critique to two foundational kalam arguments: the temporal origination argument (huduth) and the possibility argument (imkan). He claims that the universal premise 'every originated thing has an originator' is not needed to know that this world has an originator. Likewise, the universal premise 'every possible needs a preponderator' is not needed to know that this possible world needs a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two theological arguments mentioned
The huduth argument: 'This is originated, and every originated must have an originator, therefore this has an originator.' The imkan argument: 'This is possible, and every possible must have a preponderator, therefore this has a preponderator.'
2
Extract the universal premise in each argument
In huduth: 'every originated must have an originator'. In imkan: 'every possible must have a preponderator'. These are the universal premises that the logicians claim are essential.
3
Note Ibn Taymiyya's claim about knowledge of individuals
Ibn Taymiyya states that 'knowledge of its individuals is possible [without it]', meaning one can know the conclusion about a particular thing without the universal premise.
4
Understand the implication for demonstrative necessity
If the particular conclusion (this world is originated and needs a creator) is known directly, then the universal premise is not needed for that knowledge. The syllogistic form is not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال ذلك أنه إذا أريد إبطال قول من يثبت الأحوال ويقول إنها لا موجودة ولا معدومة فقيل هذان نقيضان وكل نقيضين لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان فإن هذا جعل للواحد لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً ولا يمكن جعل شيء من الأشياء لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً في حال واحدة فلا يمكن جعل الحال لا موجودة ولا معدومة كان العلم بأن هذا المعين لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً ممكناً بدون هذه القضية الكلية فلا يفتقر العلم بالنتيجة إلى البرهان وكذلك إذا قيل إن هذا ممكن وكل ممكن فلا بد له من مرجح لوجوده على عدمه على أصح القولين أو الأحد طرفيه على قول طائفة من الناس أو قيل هذا محدث وكل محدث فلا بد له من محدث فتلك القضية الكلية وهي قولنا كل محدث لا بد له من محدث وكل ممكن لا بد له من مرجح يمكن العلم بأفرادها
Example of that: when one intends to refute the position of those who affirm 'states' (al-aḥwāl) and say they are neither existent nor non-existent, and it is said: 'These two are contradictories, and every two contradictories neither coexist nor both vanish'—for this makes the one [thing] neither existent nor non-existent, and it is impossible to make any thing neither existent nor non-existent in a single state, so it is impossible to make a 'state' neither existent nor non-existent—knowledge that this particular thing cannot be existent and non-existent is possible without this universal proposition, so knowledge of the conclusion does not depend on demonstration. Likewise, when it is said: 'This is possible, and every possible must have a preponderating factor for its existence over its non-existence'—according to the more correct of the two opinions, or 'a preponderator of one of its two sides' according to a group of people—or it is said: 'This is originated (ḥādith), and every originated must have an originator'—that universal proposition, namely our saying 'every originated must have an originator' and 'every possible must have a preponderator', knowledge of its individuals is possible [without it].
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage provides two concrete examples from Islamic theology (kalām) to illustrate Ibn Taymiyya's earlier claim that certain knowledge of particulars does not require universal premises or syllogistic demonstration. The first example addresses the proponents of 'states' (aḥwāl), a theological position that posits entities that are neither existent nor non-existent. The refutation typically uses a syllogism: 'These two (existent and non-existent) are contradictories; every two contradictories cannot coexist and cannot both vanish; therefore, a state cannot be simultaneously existent and non-existent.' Ibn Taymiyya argues that one already knows that this particular thing cannot be both existent and non-existent directly, without the universal premise 'every two contradictories neither coexist nor both vanish.' The universal is unnecessary because the particular knowledge is immediate. The second example involves two classic kalām arguments: the possibility argument (that a possible being needs a preponderator) and the temporal origination argument (that an originated thing needs an originator). In each case, the logicians claim that the universal premise (e.g., 'every originated must have an originator') is essential. Ibn Taymiyya counters that knowledge of the particular instance (e.g., that this world is originated and thus needs a creator) is known without the universal; one can know the particular conclusion directly. Thus, the demonstrative syllogism is not required for these theological proofs, undermining the logicians' claim of its indispensability.

المرجح

A) They are false
B) They are necessary for the arguments to be valid
C) Knowledge of the particular instances is possible without them
D) They are only needed for the possibility argument, not the huduth argument

Key Takeaway
The huduth and imkan arguments are known directly without universal premises, challenging the necessity of syllogistic demonstration.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
5 min
Knowledge of Particular Contradictories Is Immediate
Author argues that knowledge of particular contradictories does not require universal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya holds that knowing two specific opposites are contradictory does not require a universal premise.

Core Idea
Particular knowledge of opposites is direct; universal premises are unnecessary.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' assumption that all certain knowledge must be mediated by a universal premise and a syllogism. He uses the simple case of knowing that two specific opposites cannot coexist — something we know directly, without needing to formulate a universal rule. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
What is the logicians' claim that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking? They assert that knowledge of a particular fact, such as that two opposites do not coexist, requires a universal premise like 'every pair of opposites do not coexist' as part of a demonstrative syllogism.
The logicians' position restricts certain knowledge to syllogistic form.
2
Extract the counterexample
Ibn Taymiyya gives the example of knowing that a particular object is not both black and white. He says one knows this directly, without needing to first know the universal 'every thing is not black and white'.
3
Analyze the logical structure
He argues that the universal premise only provides the major premise (e.g., 'every pair of opposites do not coexist'), but the minor premise (e.g., 'these two are opposites') is needed to get the conclusion. Without the minor, the universal is useless. Moreover, the conclusion can be known without the major premise at all, as in direct perception.
This shows the universal is not necessary for the particular knowledge.
4
Evaluate the epistemological point
Ibn Taymiyya's argument implies that some knowledge is immediate and does not depend on syllogistic structure. This challenges the logicians' claim that all certain knowledge must be derived from universal premises.
This opens the door to other forms of valid reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقيضان : من يثبت الأحوال . فإن الإنسان يعلم أن هذا الشيء لا يكون أسود أبيض و لا يكون متحركاً ساكناً، كما يعلم أن الآخر كذلك . ولا يحتاج في العلم بذلك إلى قضية كلية بأن كل شيء لا يكون أسود أبيض، ولا يكون متحركاً ساكناً . وكذلك في سائر ما يعلم تضادهما. فإن علم تضاد المعينين علم أنهما لا يجتمعان. وإن لم يعلم تضادهما لم يغنه العلم بالقضية الكلية، وهي علمه بأن كل ضدين لا يجتمعان . فإن العلم بالقضية الكلية يفيد العلم بالمقدمة الكبرى المشتملة على الحد الأكبر، وذلك لا يغني بدون العلم بالمقدمة الصغرى المشتملة على الحد الأصغر. والعلم بالنتيجة - وهو أن هذين المعينين ضدان، فلا يجتمعان يمكن بدون العلم بالمقدمة الكبرى، وهو أن كل ضدين لا يجتمعان . فلم يفتقر العلم بذلك إلى القياس الذي خصوه باسم البرهان . وإن كان البرهان في كلام الله ورسوله وكلام سائر أصناف العلماء لا يختص بما يسمونه هم البرهان. وإنما خصوا هم لفظ البرهان بما اشتمل على القياس الذي خصوا صورته ومادته بما ذكروه . مثال ذلك : أنه إذا أريد إبطال قول من يثبت الأحوال ويقول: إنها لا موجودة ولا معدومة، فقيل: هذان نقيضان و كل نقيضين لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، فإن هذا جعل للواحد لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً، ولا يمكن جعل شيء من الأشياء لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً في حال واحدة فلا يمكن جعل (الحال) لا موجودة ولا معدومة . كان العلم بأن هذا المعين لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً ممكناً بدون هذه القضية الكلية، فلا يفتقر العلم بالنتيجة إلى البرهان .
The two contradictories: Whoever affirms the states. For man knows that this thing is not black and white, and is not moving and stationary, just as he knows that the other is likewise. And he does not need in knowing that a universal proposition that every thing is not black and white, and is not moving and stationary. Likewise in the rest of what is known of their opposition. For if he knows the opposition of two specific things, he knows that they do not coexist. And if he does not know their opposition, the knowledge of the universal proposition—which is his knowledge that every pair of opposites do not coexist—does not suffice him. For knowledge of the universal proposition yields knowledge of the major premise containing the major term, and that does not suffice without knowledge of the minor premise containing the minor term. And knowledge of the conclusion—which is that these two specific things are opposites, so they do not coexist—is possible without knowledge of the major premise, which is that every pair of opposites do not coexist. Thus knowledge of that does not depend on the syllogism which they specifically call demonstrative. And although demonstrative proof (burhan) in the speech of Allah, His Messenger, and speech of all kinds of scholars is not restricted to what they call burhan. Rather, they specifically restricted the term burhan to what includes the syllogism whose form and matter they specified as they mentioned. Example of that: If one wants to refute the statement of those who affirm 'ahwal' (states) and say: 'They are neither existent nor non-existent', and it is said: 'These two are contradictories, and every pair of contradictories neither coexist nor are both removed', then this makes the single thing neither existent nor non-existent, and it is not possible to make anything neither existent nor non-existent at one moment, so it is not possible to make the 'hal' neither existent nor non-existent. So knowledge that this specific thing is neither existent nor non-existent is possible without this universal proposition, thus knowledge of the conclusion does not depend on demonstrative proof.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that certain knowledge requires a two-premise demonstrative syllogism (burhan). He begins by defining the two contradictories (al-naqidan) and opposites (al-diddan). He asserts that a person knows directly that a particular object is not both black and white or both moving and stationary, without needing to formulate a universal premise like 'every thing is not both black and white'. This immediate knowledge of specific opposition does not require the universal. He then clarifies that if one does not know the opposition of two specific things, the universal premise alone is useless — it only gives the major premise without the minor. The conclusion (e.g., 'these two are opposites') can be known without the major premise. Therefore, such knowledge does not need the logicians' specialized syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya also notes that the term 'burhan' is not exclusive to the logicians' technical definition; it is used broadly in Islamic discourse. He provides an example: the refutation of those who affirm 'ahwal' (states) as neither existent nor non-existent. The argument uses the principle that contradictories cannot coexist nor both be absent. From this, the particular conclusion (that a state cannot be neither existent nor non-existent) is known without needing the universal premise 'every pair of contradictories cannot coexist nor both be absent' as a separate major premise. The knowledge is immediate. This reinforces his critique that the logicians' formal structure is unnecessary for valid reasoning.

Key Terms
النقيضان
الضدان
القضية الكلية
الحد الأكبر
الحد الأصغر
المقدمة الكبرى
المقدمة الصغرى

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does knowledge that two specific opposites do not coexist not depend on the universal premise that every pair of opposites do not coexist?
A) Because the universal premise is false.
B) Because knowledge of the particular can be immediate and the universal premise alone is useless without the minor premise.
C) Because opposites are not really contradictory.
D) Because logicians never used universal premises.

Key Takeaway
Particular knowledge of opposites can be immediate; universal premises are not necessary for all certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the statement: 'Wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating.' Is it necessary to first know the universal 'every intoxicant is forbidden' before knowing that wine is forbidden? Explain why or why not using Ibn Taymiyya's argument.
?
2. In a fiqh debate on riba, a jurist says: 'This transaction is riba because it involves excess and delay.' Would the logicians say this requires a universal premise? How would Ibn Taymiyya respond?

Demonstration -- Burhan
