The 'Ayah': Direct Sign vs. Syllogistic Reasoning
Defining 'ayah' as direct sign versus syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's concept of 'ayah' as a direct sign from syllogistic reasoning (qiyas) and identify its three examples from the passage.

Core Idea
A direct sign (ayah) gives knowledge of the specific thing itself, not a universal.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya introduces a crucial distinction between two types of reasoning: the direct sign (ayah) and syllogistic analogy (qiyas). He argues that Quranic reasoning often employs ayat that directly entail their specific objects, bypassing the need for universal premises. We will examine his definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of an 'ayah'
Ibn Taymiyya says the sign entails 'ayn al-madlul (the very thing signified), not a universal concept shared among multiple things. Find that definition in the Arabic.
2
Examine the sun-daytime example
The sun is a sign of daytime: knowledge of the sunrise directly gives knowledge of daytime, not a universal principle. This illustrates direct entailment.
Quran 17:12 is cited as evidence.
3
Apply to signs of prophethood
The signs of Muhammad's prophethood directly entail his prophethood, not a universal quality shared with other prophets or claims.
4
Apply to signs of God
Similarly, signs of God (e.g., creation) directly yield knowledge of His essence, not a universal concept of divinity.
The ayah method avoids the need for a middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال بـ «الآيات» في القرآن. بيان الآية: وأما استدلاله تعالى بالآيات فكثير في القرآن، والفرقُ بين الآيات وبين القياس أن العلامة، وهي الدليل الذي يستلزم عين المدلول، لا يكون مدلوله أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بين المطلوب وغيره، بل نفس العلم به يوجب العلم بعين المدلول، كما أن الشمس آية النهار. قال تعالى: ﴿ وَجَعَلْنَا الَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ آيَتَيْنِ فَمَحَوْنَا آيَةَ الَّيْلِ وَجَعَلْنَا آيَةَ النَّهَارِ مُبْصِرَةً ﴾ [سورة الإسراء: 12]. فنفس العلم بطلوع الشمس يوجب العلم بوجود النهار. وكذلك آياتُ نبوّة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم نفس العلم بها يوجب العلم بنبوته، ولا يوجب أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره. وكذلك آياتُ الربّ تعالى نفس العلم بها يوجب العلم بنفسه المقدسة تعالى، ولا يوجب علماً كلياً مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره. والعلم بكون هذا مستلزماً لهذا هو جهة الدليل. فكل دليل في الوجود لا بد أن يكون مستلزماً للمدلول، والعلم باستلزام التلازم المعيَّن للمعيَّن المطلوب أقرب إلى الفطرة من العلم بأن كل معين من معينات القضية الكلية يستلزم النتيجة. والقضايا الكلية هذا شأنها، فإن القضايا الكلية إن لم تعلم معيناتها بغير التمثيل، وإلا لم تعلم إلا بالتمثيل. فلا بد من معرفة لزوم المدلول للدليل الذي هو الحد الأوسط. فلا بد أن يعرف أن كل فرد من أفراد الحكم الكلي المطلوب يلزم كل فرد من أفراد الدليل. كما إذا قيل: كل أ ب، وكل ب ج، فكل ج أ. فإذا كان كلياً فلا بد أن يعرف أن كل فرد من أفراد الجيم يلزم كل فرد من أفراد الباء، وكل فرد من أفراد الباء يلزم كل فرد من أفراد الألف. ومعلوم أن العلم بلزوم الجيم المعين للباء المعين، والباء المعين للألف المعين، أقرب إلى الفطرة من هذا. وهذا كما قدمناه في أمثلة أقيستهم البرهانية، مثل قولهم: «الكل أعظم من الجزء»، و«الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية»، و«الضدان لا يجتمعان»، و«النقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان»، ونحو ذلك. فإن هذه قضايا كلية. ومعلوم أن الإنسان إذا تصور ما يتصوره من شيء معين وجزئه، فإن تصوره لكون هذا الكل المعيَّن أعظم من جزئه أسبقُ إلى عقله من أن يتخيل أن «كل كل أعظم من جزئه». فهو يتصور أن بدنه أعظم من يده ورجله، وأن السماءَ أعظم من كواكبها، والجبل أعظم من بعضه، والمدينة أعظم من بعضها، ونحو ذلك، قبل أن يتصور القضية الكلية الشاملة لجميع هذه الأفراد. وكذلك إذا تصور شيئاً معيناً يعلم أنه لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً في حال واحدة، يتصور أن «كل نقيضين لا يجتمعان». وكذلك إذا تصور سواداً معيناً علم أنه لا يكون اللون الواحد سواداً بياضاً قبل أن يتصور أن «كل ضدّين لا يجتمعان». وأمثال ذلك كثيرة. وإذا قيل: تلك القضية الكلية تحصل في الذهن ضرورة أو بديهة من واهب العقل، قيل: فحصول تلك القضية المعينة في الذهن من واهب العقل أقرب. عِظَم الفرق بين إثبات الرب بالآيات وبين إثباته بالقياس البرهاني: ومعلوم أن كل ما سوى الله من الممكنات فإنه مستلزم لذات الرب تعالى - يمتنع وجوده بدون وجود ذات الرب تعالى وتقدَّس. وإن كان مستلزماً أيضاً لأمور كلية مشتركة بينه وبين غيره، فلأنه يلزم من وجوده وجود لوازمه، وتلك الكليات المشتركة من لوازم المعين - أعنى يلزمه ما يخصه من ذلك الكلي العام والكلي المشترك - يلزمه بشرط وجوده، ووجود العالم الذي يتصور القدر المشترك. وهو سبحانه يعلم الأمور على ما هي عليه، فيعلم نفسه المقدسة بما يخصها، ويعلم الكليات أنها كليات، فيلزم من وجود الخاص وجود العام المطلق، أي حصة المعيَّن من ذلك العام، كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان وجود «الإنسان».
Reasoning through 'signs' in the Quran. Explanation of the sign: As for the Almighty's reasoning through signs, it is abundant in the Quran. The difference between signs and analogy is that the sign—which is the indication that entails the very thing signified—its signified is not a universal matter shared between the sought and other than it, rather knowledge of it itself necessitates knowledge of the very thing signified, just as the sun is a sign of daytime. He said: ﴿And We made the night and day two signs, and We blotted out the sign of the night and made the sign of the day visible﴾ [Surah al-Isra: 12]. Thus knowledge of the sunrise itself necessitates knowledge of the existence of daytime. Likewise, the signs of the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him)—knowledge of them themselves necessitates knowledge of his prophethood, and does not necessitate a universal matter shared between him and other than him. Likewise, the signs of the Lord Almighty—knowledge of them themselves necessitates knowledge of His sacred Self, and does not necessitate universal knowledge shared between Him and other than Him. The knowledge that this is entailed by that is the aspect of the indication. So every indication in existence must necessarily entail the indicated, and knowledge of the entailment of the specific entailment for the specific sought is closer to innate disposition than knowledge that every specific instance of the universal proposition entails the conclusion. The universal propositions have this property: if their specific instances are not known except by example, then they are not known except by example. So it is necessary to know the entailment of the indicated by the indication which is the middle term. So it must be known that every individual of the universal sought judgment entails every individual of the indication. As when it is said: every A is B, and every B is C, so every C is A. If it is universal, then it must be known that every individual of C entails every individual of B, and every individual of B entails every individual of A. It is known that knowledge of the entailment of the specific C for the specific B, and the specific B for the specific A, is closer to innate disposition than this. This is as we have presented in the examples of their demonstrative syllogisms, such as their saying: 'the whole is greater than the part', and 'things equal to one thing are equal to each other', and 'two contraries cannot coexist', and 'two contradictories cannot coexist nor both be absent', and the like. These are universal propositions. It is known that when a person imagines what he imagines of a specific thing and its part, his imagination that this specific whole is greater than its part is more immediate to his mind than his imagining that 'every whole is greater than its part'. For he imagines that his body is greater than his hand and foot, that the sky is greater than its planets, the mountain is greater than part of it, the city is greater than part of it, and so on, before he imagines the universal proposition encompassing all these individuals. Likewise, when he imagines a specific thing, he knows that it cannot exist and be nonexistent in one state; he imagines that 'every two contradictories cannot coexist'. Likewise, when he imagines a specific blackness, he knows that a single color cannot be both black and white before he imagines that 'every two contraries cannot coexist'. And many such examples. If it is said: that universal proposition comes to mind necessarily or intuitively from the bestower of intellect, then it is said: the coming to mind of that specific proposition from the bestower of intellect is more immediate. The great difference between establishing the Lord through signs and establishing Him through demonstrative syllogism: It is known that everything other than God among contingents entails the Self of the Lord Almighty—its existence is impossible without the existence of the Self of the Lord Almighty, exalted is He. And if it also entails universal matters shared between it and other than it, then that is because from its existence follows the existence of its consequences, and those shared universals are among the consequences of the specific—I mean, what pertains specifically to it from that universal genus and the shared universal—it entails them conditionally upon its existence, and the existence of the world that conceives the common aspect. And He, glorified, knows things as they are; He knows His sacred Self by what is specific to it, and He knows universals as universals. So from the existence of the specific follows the existence of the absolute universal, i.e., the portion of the specific from that universal, just as from the existence of 'this human' follows the existence of 'human'.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya on the distinction between two types of reasoning: reasoning by signs (ayat) and reasoning by syllogistic analogy (qiyas), particularly in the context of Quranic proofs. He begins by defining the 'ayah' (sign) as an indication that directly entails the very thing signified, not a universal concept shared among multiple referents. For example, the sun is a sign of daytime: knowledge of the sunrise directly gives knowledge of daytime, not a universal proposition about 'if sun then day'. He cites Quran 17:12 and extends this to the signs of prophethood and of God Himself: the specific signs directly yield knowledge of the specific thing signified, not a universal shared with others.

Ibn Taymiyya then contrasts this with the method of the logicians, who rely on universal premises and the middle term. He argues that knowledge of specific entailments (e.g., this particular whole is greater than its part) is more primordial and innate (fitra) than knowledge of the universal proposition (e.g., 'every whole is greater than its part'). He illustrates with examples like the whole-part relation, equality, contraries, and contradictories, showing that humans first grasp specific cases before universalizing.

He addresses the possible objection that universal propositions are innate from the Bestower of Intellect, responding that specific propositions are even more so. Finally, he applies this to the proof of God: all contingent things entail the existence of God specifically, not merely through a universal concept. God's knowledge encompasses both the specific and the universal, but the specific is more fundamental. Thus, the Quranic method of 'ayat' is superior to demonstrative syllogism in establishing the existence of God.

Key Terms
الآية
القياس
المدلول

According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the key difference between an ayah and qiyas?

B) Ayah gives knowledge of a specific thing without a universal, while qiyas uses universal premises.
C) Ayah is only for natural phenomena, qiyas for theology.

Key Takeaway
An ayah (sign) is a direct indication that yields knowledge of the specific thing signified, without reliance on universal concepts or syllogistic form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Specific Entailments: The Primacy of Particular Knowledge
Arguing for the priority of specific entailments over universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's argument that knowledge of specific entailments is more fundamental than universal propositions, and apply it to examples like whole-part and contraries.

Core Idea
Specific entailments are known by fitra before universal rules.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique by arguing that our knowledge of particular entailments (e.g., this whole is greater than its part) is more immediate and innate than the corresponding universal proposition (every whole is greater than its part). He supports this with examples from everyday reasoning and even addresses the objection that universals are divinely bestowed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Ibn Taymiyya states that knowledge of a specific entailment (talazum mu'ayyan) is closer to fitra than knowledge of a universal proposition. Locate this claim in the Arabic.
2
Examine the whole-part example
A person knows that his body is greater than his hand before knowing the universal that every whole is greater than its part. This shows the priority of specific knowledge.
3
Examine the contraries example
Knowledge that a particular color cannot be both black and white precedes the universal rule about contraries.
4
Address the objection
If one says universals come from the Bestower of Intellect, Ibn Taymiyya retorts that specific propositions come even more directly.

كما إذا قيل: كل أ ب، وكل ب ج، فكل ج أ. فإذا كان كلياً فلا بد أن يعرف أن كل فرد من أفراد الجيم يلزم كل فرد من أفراد الباء، وكل فرد من أفراد الباء يلزم كل فرد من أفراد الألف. ومعلوم أن العلم بلزوم الجيم المعين للباء المعين، والباء المعين للألف المعين، أقرب إلى الفطرة من هذا. وهذا كما قدمناه في أمثلة أقيستهم البرهانية، مثل قولهم: «الكل أعظم من الجزء»، و«الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية»، و«الضدان لا يجتمعان»، و«النقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان»، ونحو ذلك. فإن هذه قضايا كلية. ومعلوم أن الإنسان إذا تصور ما يتصوره من شيء معين وجزئه، فإن تصوره لكون هذا الكل المعيَّن أعظم من جزئه أسبقُ إلى عقله من أن يتخيل أن «كل كل أعظم من جزئه». فهو يتصور أن بدنه أعظم من يده ورجله، وأن السماءَ أعظم من كواكبها، والجبل أعظم من بعضه، والمدينة أعظم من بعضها، ونحو ذلك، قبل أن يتصور القضية الكلية الشاملة لجميع هذه الأفراد. وكذلك إذا تصور شيئاً معيناً يعلم أنه لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً في حال واحدة، يتصور أن «كل نقيضين لا يجتمعان». وكذلك إذا تصور سواداً معيناً علم أنه لا يكون اللون الواحد سواداً بياضاً قبل أن يتصور أن «كل ضدّين لا يجتمعان». وأمثال ذلك كثيرة. وإذا قيل: تلك القضية الكلية تحصل في الذهن ضرورة أو بديهة من واهب العقل، قيل: فحصول تلك القضية المعينة في الذهن من واهب العقل أقرب. عِظَم الفرق بين إثبات الرب بالآيات وبين إثباته بالقياس البرهاني: ومعلوم أن كل ما سوى الله من الممكنات فإنه مستلزم لذات الرب تعالى - يمتنع وجوده بدون وجود ذات الرب تعالى وتقدَّس. وإن كان مستلزماً أيضاً لأمور كلية مشتركة بينه وبين غيره، فلأنه يلزم من وجوده وجود لوازمه، وتلك الكليات المشتركة من لوازم المعين - أعنى يلزمه ما يخصه من ذلك الكلي العام والكلي المشترك - يلزمه بشرط وجوده، ووجود العالم الذي يتصور القدر المشترك. وهو سبحانه يعلم الأمور على ما هي عليه، فيعلم نفسه المقدسة بما يخصها، ويعلم الكليات أنها كليات، فيلزم من وجود الخاص وجود العام المطلق، أي حصة المعيَّن من ذلك العام، كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان وجود «الإنسان».
Reasoning through 'signs' in the Quran. Explanation of the sign: As for the Almighty's reasoning through signs, it is abundant in the Quran. The difference between signs and analogy is that the sign—which is the indication that entails the very thing signified—its signified is not a universal matter shared between the sought and other than it, rather knowledge of it itself necessitates knowledge of the very thing signified, just as the sun is a sign of daytime. He said: ﴿And We made the night and day two signs, and We blotted out the sign of the night and made the sign of the day visible﴾ [Surah al-Isra: 12]. Thus knowledge of the sunrise itself necessitates knowledge of the existence of daytime. Likewise, the signs of the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him)—knowledge of them themselves necessitates knowledge of his prophethood, and does not necessitate a universal matter shared between him and other than him. Likewise, the signs of the Lord Almighty—knowledge of them themselves necessitates knowledge of His sacred Self, and does not necessitate universal knowledge shared between Him and other than Him. The knowledge that this is entailed by that is the aspect of the indication. So every indication in existence must necessarily entail the indicated, and knowledge of the entailment of the specific entailment for the specific sought is closer to innate disposition than knowledge that every specific instance of the universal proposition entails the conclusion. The universal propositions have this property: if their specific instances are not known except by example, then they are not known except by example. So it is necessary to know the entailment of the indicated by the indication which is the middle term. So it must be known that every individual of the universal sought judgment entails every individual of the indication. As when it is said: every A is B, and every B is C, so every C is A. If it is universal, then it must be known that every individual of C entails every individual of B, and every individual of B entails every individual of A. It is known that knowledge of the entailment of the specific C for the specific B, and the specific B for the specific A, is closer to innate disposition than this. This is as we have presented in the examples of their demonstrative syllogisms, such as their saying: 'the whole is greater than the part', and 'things equal to one thing are equal to each other', and 'two contraries cannot coexist', and 'two contradictories cannot coexist nor both be absent', and the like. These are universal propositions. It is known that when a person imagines what he imagines of a specific thing and its part, his imagination that this specific whole is greater than its part is more immediate to his mind than his imagining that 'every whole is greater than its part'. For he imagines that his body is greater than his hand and foot, that the sky is greater than its planets, the mountain is greater than part of it, the city is greater than part of it, and so on, before he imagines the universal proposition encompassing all these individuals. Likewise, when he imagines a specific thing, he knows that it cannot exist and be nonexistent in one state; he imagines that 'every two contradictories cannot coexist'. Likewise, when he imagines a specific blackness, he knows that a single color cannot be both black and white before he imagines that 'every two contraries cannot coexist'. And many such examples. If it is said: that universal proposition comes to mind necessarily or intuitively from the bestower of intellect, then it is said: the coming to mind of that specific proposition from the bestower of intellect is more immediate. The great difference between establishing the Lord through signs and establishing Him through demonstrative syllogism: It is known that everything other than God among contingents entails the Self of the Lord Almighty—its existence is impossible without the existence of the Self of the Lord Almighty, exalted is He. And if it also entails universal matters shared between it and other than it, then that is because from its existence follows the existence of its consequences, and those shared universals are among the consequences of the specific—I mean, what pertains specifically to it from that universal genus and the shared universal—it entails them conditionally upon its existence, and the existence of the world that conceives the common aspect. And He, glorified, knows things as they are; He knows His sacred Self by what is specific to it, and He knows universals as universals. So from the existence of the specific follows the existence of the absolute universal, i.e., the portion of the specific from that universal, just as from the existence of 'this human' follows the existence of 'human'.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya on the distinction between two types of reasoning: reasoning by signs (ayat) and reasoning by syllogistic analogy (qiyas), particularly in the context of Quranic proofs. He begins by defining the 'ayah' (sign) as an indication that directly entails the very thing signified, not a universal concept shared among multiple referents. For example, the sun is a sign of daytime: knowledge of the sunrise directly gives knowledge of daytime, not a universal proposition about 'if sun then day'. He cites Quran 17:12 and extends this to the signs of prophethood and of God Himself: the specific signs directly yield knowledge of the specific thing signified, not a universal shared with others.

Ibn Taymiyya then contrasts this with the method of the logicians, who rely on universal premises and the middle term. He argues that knowledge of specific entailments (e.g., this particular whole is greater than its part) is more primordial and innate (fitra) than knowledge of the universal proposition (e.g., 'every whole is greater than its part'). He illustrates with examples like the whole-part relation, equality, contraries, and contradictories, showing that humans first grasp specific cases before universalizing.

He addresses the possible objection that universal propositions are innate from the Bestower of Intellect, responding that specific propositions are even more so. Finally, he applies this to the proof of God: all contingent things entail the existence of God specifically, not merely through a universal concept. God's knowledge encompasses both the specific and the universal, but the specific is more fundamental. Thus, the Quranic method of 'ayat' is superior to demonstrative syllogism in establishing the existence of God.

Key Terms
التلازم المعين
القضايا الكلية
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ibn Taymiyya argues that knowledge of specific entailments (e.g., this whole is greater than that part) is more basic and innate than knowledge of universal propositions.

Qiyas al-Awla: The Analogical Method for Divine Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya presents qiyas al-awla as the method of the salaf for affirming God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain qiyas al-awla and apply it to affirm a divine attribute such as knowledge or power.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-awla affirms God's perfections by analogy, with infinite superiority beyond intellectual grasp.

How do we know that God possesses attributes like knowledge, power, and mercy when these terms are also used for creatures? The salaf used a special form of reasoning called qiyas al-awla (the argument from the worthier), which is rooted in the Quran. In this lesson, you will learn how this method works and why it avoids both anthropomorphism and denial of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method being described
In the passage, Ibn Taymiyya introduces 'قياس الأولى' (qiyas al-awla) as the method of the salaf, following the Quran. Notice that it is opposed to the flawed syllogism of the philosophers. Mark the key phrase: 'يدل على أنه يثبت له من صفات الكمال التي لا نقص فيها أكمل مما علموه ثابتاً لغيره'.
2
Understand the analogical structure
The method takes a perfection known in creation (e.g., knowledge in a scholar) and affirms for God a more perfect version of that attribute. The reasoning is analogical: the creature's attribute is a shadow of God's attribute. The phrase 'مع التفاوت الذي لا يضبطه العقل' shows that the exact degree of superiority is unknowable.
This is not a categorical syllogism but a special analogy (qiyas).
3
Grasp the epistemological limitation
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that even though we know the genus جنس of the attribute (e.g., knowledge), the specific reality of God's attribute is unique and beyond reason. The dissimilarity between Creator and creature is incommensurable. This prevents anthropomorphism while affirming real attributes.
The intellect knows the genus but not the exact modality (كيفية).
4
Apply the method to a sample attribute
Consider the attribute 'mercy'. In creation, mercy is a perfection. By qiyas al-awla, we affirm that God's mercy is infinitely more perfect, but we cannot quantify it. This is the method used by the salaf in interpreting verses like 'ولله المثل الأعلى' (To God is the highest attribute).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلالة «قياس الأولى» في إثبات صفات الكمال من آياته. وأما «قياس الأولى الذي كان يسلكه السلفُ اتباعاً للقرآن فيدل على أنه يثبت له من صفات الكمال التي لا نقص فيها أكمل مما علموه ثابتاً لغيره، مع التفاوت الذي لا يضبطه العقل، كما لا يضبط التفاوت بين الخالق وبين المخلوق. بل إذا كان العقل يدرك من التفاضيل التي بين مخلوق ومخلوق ما لا يُخصَر قدره، وهو يعلم أن فضل الله على كل مخلوق أعظم من فضل مخلوق على مخلوق كان هذا مما يبين له أن ما يثبت للرب أعظم مما يثبت لكل ما سواه بما لا يدرك قدره. فكأن قياس الأولى يفيده أمراً يختص به الربّ، مع علمه بجنس ذلك الأمر.
The indication of 'the argument from the worthier' in affirming the attributes of perfection from His signs. As for 'the argument from the worthier' which the predecessors used, following the Quran, it indicates that He affirms for Him, from the attributes of perfection that have no deficiency, that which is more perfect than what they know to be affirmed for others, with a disparity that the intellect cannot grasp, just as it cannot grasp the disparity between the Creator and the creature. Rather, if the intellect perceives of the distinctions between one creature and another what is inestimable, and it knows that the superiority of God over every creature is greater than the superiority of one creature over another, then this shows that what is affirmed for the Lord is greater than what is affirmed for everything else to an inestimable degree. So the argument from the worthier seems to give him a matter specific to the Lord, while he knows the genus of that matter.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines and explains qiyas al-awla, which is the correct method for affirming God's attributes according to Ibn Taymiyya, following the salaf. He contrasts it with the flawed syllogism of the philosophers. The method works by taking known perfections in creation that are free from any deficiency and affirming for God a more perfect version of them. However, the exact degree of superiority is beyond intellectual grasp. For example, we know that knowledge in a scholar is a perfection; by qiyas al-awla, we affirm that God's knowledge is infinitely more perfect. The disparity between the Creator's attribute and the creature's attribute is incommensurable, just as the disparity between the Creator and the creature in essence is incommensurable. This method, derived from Quranic reasoning (e.g., 'To God is the highest attribute'), allows the intellect to know the genus of the attribute (e.g., knowledge, power) while acknowledging that the specific reality of God's attribute is unique and beyond comprehension. Thus, qiyas al-awla correctly affirms attributes specific to God without falling into anthropomorphism or denial.

Key Terms
قياس الأولى
صفات الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) That they are known through categorical syllogism alone.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-awla is a Quranic analogical method that affirms God's attributes of perfection in a superior degree while recognizing that the exact superiority is beyond intellectual comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Compare qiyas al-awla with a categorical syllogism. Write a short paragraph explaining why the syllogism 'All perfections belong to God; knowledge is a perfection; therefore God has knowledge' is insufficient for theology, and how qiyas al-awla adds the crucial element of superior degree.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Limits of Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that their demonstrative syllogism is the only way to theoretical knowledge, and attacks their view on God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the logicians' exclusive claim for demonstration and identify his critique against applying syllogism to God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the claim that burhan is the only path to knowledge, especially of God.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya delivers a stinging critique of the logicians' overreach. Having already shown that their demonstrative syllogism fails to achieve the ultimate objective—knowledge of God—he now exposes the audacity of their claim that even God's knowledge is obtained via syllogism. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for the limits of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims under attack
First, the logicians' negative proposition that theoretical sciences are only obtained by burhan. Second, their extreme claim that knowledge of God and His prophets is obtained via syllogism involving a middle term.
These are distinct but related: the second extends the first to divine knowledge.
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's evaluative language
Notice words like 'شناعة' (heinousness) and 'تحجروا' (they confined). These signal his strong disapproval and rhetorical strategy.
Ibn Taymiyya uses emotionally charged terms to highlight the gravity of the error.
3
Examine his comparison with earlier thinkers
He compares Ibn Sina's followers to those who denied God's knowledge entirely, and even to Jews and Christians. He argues that the logicians are more astray because they have a method that produces worthless results.
This comparison emphasizes that even minimal faith is better than misguided logic.
4
Extract the epistemological objection
The core objection: the most important knowledge (of God) is not obtained through middle terms but through revelation and divine signs. The logicians' method is insufficient for this purpose.
This rejects the exclusive epistemic authority of burhan for ultimate matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتبين بذلك إن قياسهم البرهاني لا يحصِّلُ المطلوب الذي به تكمل النفس في معرفة «الموجودات» ومعرفة خالقها، فضلاً عن أن يقال : «لا تُعلم المَطَالبُ إِلا به». وهذا باب واسع لكن المقصود في هذا المقام التنبيه على بطلان قضيتهم السالبة ، وهي قولهم : «إن العلوم النظرية لا تحصل إلا بواسطة برهانهم .
شناعة زَعْمِهم أن علم الله أيضاً يحصل بواسطة «القياس»
ثم لم يَكْفِهم هذا السلبُ العام الذي تحجّروا فيه واسعاً، وقصروا العلوم على طريق ضيفة لا تحصل إلا مطلوباً لا طائل فيه حتى زعموا أن علم الله وعلم أنبيائه وأوليائه إنما يحصل بواسطة القياس» المشتمل على الحد الأوسط»، كما يذكر ذلك ابن سينا وأتباعه .
وهم في إثبات ذلك خير ممن نفى عِلمه وعِلمَ أنبيائه من سلفهم الذين هم من أجهل الناس برب العالمين، وبأنبيائه، وبكتبه.
فابن سينا لَمَّا تميز عن أولئك بمزيد عقل وعلم سلك طريقهم المنطقي في تقرير ذلك. وصاروا سالكوا هذه الطريق، وإن كانوا أعلم من سلفهم وأكمل، فهم أضلُّ من اليهود والنصارى وأجهلُ ، إذ كان أولئك حَصَل لهم من الإيمان بـ «واجب الوجود».
Thus it became clear that their demonstrative syllogism does not achieve the sought objective by which the soul is perfected in the knowledge of 'existents' and the knowledge of their Creator, let alone that it be said: 'Theoretical questions are not known except by it.' This is a broad subject, but the intent in this station is to alert to the falsehood of their negative proposition, which is their statement: 'Theoretical sciences are not obtained except by means of their demonstration.'
The heinousness of their claim that the knowledge of God also is obtained by means of 'syllogism'.
Then their general negation was not enough for them, by which they confined a wide scope and restricted sciences to a narrow path that yields nothing except a worthless sought objective, until they claimed that the knowledge of God and the knowledge of His prophets and saints is only obtained by means of the 'syllogism' that includes the 'middle term', as Ibn Sina and his followers mention.
And they, in affirming that, are better than those who denied His knowledge and the knowledge of His prophets among their predecessors, who are among the most ignorant of people concerning the Lord of the worlds, His prophets, and His books.
Ibn Sina, when he distinguished himself from those by superior intellect and knowledge, followed their logical path in establishing that. And they became followers of this path, and even if they are more knowledgeable and complete than their predecessors, they are more astray than the Jews and Christians and more ignorant, since those had attained some faith in the 'Necessary Existent'.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that their demonstrative syllogism (qiyas burhani) is the exclusive method for obtaining theoretical knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that it has been shown (from the previous discussion) that the logicians' burhan does not achieve the ultimate objective—the perfection of the soul in knowing existents and the Creator. Therefore, their claim that 'theoretical questions are not known except by it' is false. He notes this is a broad topic, but here he aims to highlight the falsehood of their negative proposition: that theoretical sciences only come through their burhan.

Then he attacks a more extreme claim: that even God's knowledge and the knowledge of His prophets and friends is obtained via syllogism involving a middle term, as asserted by Ibn Sina and his followers. Ibn Taymiyya sarcastically calls this 'heinous' (shanā'a). He criticizes these logicians for confining sciences to a narrow path that produces worthless results. He compares them to earlier thinkers who denied God's knowledge entirely—those were more ignorant but still had some faith in the Necessary Existent. Ibn Sina, despite his intelligence, adopted this logical path of his predecessors and is even more astray than Jews and Christians because the latter at least had some belief in the Necessary Existent, while Ibn Sina's logical method undermines true knowledge of God.

This passage serves as a sharp critique of the overreach of logical method in theology, emphasizing that the most important knowledge (of God) is not obtained through syllogistic demonstration but through divine signs and revelation.

Key Terms
القياس البرهاني
الحد الأوسط
القضية السالبة
واجب الوجود

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that demonstration is the exclusive path to knowledge, especially regarding knowledge of God, arguing it is heinous and leads to greater error.

Ibn Taymiyya's A Fortiori Refutation of Burhan Exclusivity
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that divine knowledge is via qiyas burhani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's a fortiori argument against the claim that knowledge is only via burhan and apply it to analogous epistemological claims in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
If burhan is not necessary for human knowledge, it certainly is not for Allah's knowledge.

In this passage from his Radd al-Mantiqiyyin, Ibn Taymiyya attacks a core epistemological claim of the logicians: that certain knowledge (al-'ilm) is only obtained through the demonstrative syllogism (burhan) they have defined. He uses a powerful argument from the lesser to the greater (a fortiori, or qiyas al-awlawi) to show that this claim fails even for ordinary humans, so it cannot apply to Allah, the angels, or the prophets. The passage also frames the logicians as arrogant followers of Pharaoh, citing several Qur'anic verses about those who reject divine signs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Read the last paragraph carefully: the logicians claim that all knowledge, including Allah's and the prophets', is obtained through the demonstrative syllogism (al-qiyas al-burhani). Find the sentence that states this.
The claim is that knowledge does not occur except through the burhan they described.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's Qur'anic framing
Ibn Taymiyya cites verses about Pharaoh and others who argued against Allah's signs without authority. How does this framing serve to delegitimize the logicians' claim?
He links their claim to arrogance (kibr) and opposition to the messengers.
3
Locate the a fortiori argument
Find the sentence that contrasts human knowledge with divine knowledge. It says: 'If this negation is false in the knowledge of individual people, then its falsehood is even more so in the knowledge of the Lord of the worlds.'
The argument moves from the lesser (humans) to the greater (Allah), a classic a fortiori (qiyas al-awlawi) structure.
4
Assess the validity of the a fortiori
Do you agree that if the claim fails for humans, it must fail for Allah? Consider that the logicians' claim is about the method of acquiring knowledge. Is the method the same for both? Ibn Taymiyya implies that if it's false for humans (who often know without burhan), then it's even more absurd for the all-knowing Creator.
This step tests the internal logic: the argument is valid if the criterion for falsehood is universal and not subject to greater perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه السادس : القول بحصول علم الله أيضاً بواسطة القياس»
وصفاته ما لم يحصل لهؤلاء الضلال، لما في صدورهم من الكبر والخيال. وهم من أتباع فرعون وأمثاله ؛ ولهذا تجدهم لموسى ومَنْ معه من أهل الملل والشرائع متنقصين أو مُعادِين ..
/ قال الله تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُجَدِلُونَ فِي ءَايَتِ اللَّهِ بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَانٍ أَتَنَهُمْ [١٤٣ / ١٥٨
إِن فِي صُدُورِهِمْ إِلَّا كِبْرُ مَاهُم بِبَلِغِيهِ ﴾ [سورة غافر : ٥٦]. وقال : [ الَّذِينَ يُجَدِلُونَ فى : التِ اللهِ بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَن أتَنهُمْ (١) كَبُرَ مَقْتًا عِندَ اللَّهِ وَعِندَ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كَذَلِكَ يَطْبَعُ اللَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ قَلْبِ مُتَكَبْرٍ جَبَّارٍ (٢٠) [سورة غافر : ٣٥] وقال: ﴿ فَلَمَّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُم بِالْبَيِّنَتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِندَهُم مِّنَ الْعِلْمِ وَحَافَ بِهِم مَّا كَانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِمُونَ (2) فَلَمَّا رَأَوْا بَأْسَنَا قَالُوا آمَنَّا بِاللَّهِ وَحْدَهُ وَكَفَرْنَا بِمَا كُنَّا بِهِ مُشْرِكِينَ فَلَمْ يَكُ يَنفَعُهُمْ إِيمَتُهُمْ لَمَّا رَأَوْا بَأْسَنَا سُنَّتَ اللَّهِ الَّتِي قَدْ خَلَتْ فِي عِبَادِهِ وَخَسِرَ هُنَالِكَ الْكَفِرُونَ (0) [سورة غافر : ٨٣ - ٨٥] .
وقد بسط الكلام على قول فرعون ومتابعة هؤلاء له، والنمرود بن كَنْعان وأمثالهما من رؤوس الكفر والضلال ومخالفتهم لموسى وإبراهيم وغيرهما من رسل الله - صلوات الله عليهم - في مواضع .
وقد جعل الله آل إبراهيم أئمة للمؤمنين أهل الجنة وآل فرعون أئمة لأهل النار . قال تعالى: ﴿ وَاسْتَكْبَرَ هُوَ وَجُنُودُهُ فِي الْأَرْضِ بِغَيْرِ الْحَقِّ وَظَنُّوا أَنَّهُمْ إِلَيْنَا لَا يُرْجَعُونَ (٢) فَأَخَذْنَهُ وَجُنُودَهُ فَنَبَذْنَهُمْ فِي الْيَمِّ فَأنظر كيف كان عقبة الظَّالِمِينَ ) وَجَعَلْنَهُمْ أَبِمَّةً يَدْعُونَ إِلَى النَّارِ وَيَوْمَ الْقِيمَةِ لَا يُنصَرُونَ )) وَأَتْبَعْنَهُمْ فِي هَذِهِ الدُّنْيَا لَعْنَةُ وَيَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ هُم مِن الْمَقْبُوحِينَ وَلَقَدْ عَانَيْنَا مُوسَى الْكِتَابَ مِنْ بَعْدِ مَا أَهْلَكْنَا الْقُرُون الأولى بص آيرَ لِلنَّاسِ وَهُدًى وَرَحْمَةٌ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَتَذَكَّرُونَ وَمَا كُنتَ بِجَانِبِ الْعَرْنِ إِذْ قَضَيْنَا إِلَى مُوسَى الْأَمْرَ وَمَا كُنتَ مِنَ الشَّهِدِينَ الله وَلَكِنا أَنشَأْنَا قُرُونًا فَنَطَاوَلَ عَلَيْهِمُ الْعُمُرُ وَمَا كُنتَ نَاوِيَا فِى أَهْلِ مَدْيَنَ تَتْلُوا عَلَيْهِمْ وَايَتِنَا وَلَكِنَّا كُنَّا مُرْسِلِينَ ) وَمَا كُنتَ بِجَانِبِ الطُّورِ إِذْ نَادَيْنَا وَلَكِن رَحْمَةً مِّن رَّبِّكَ لِتُنذِرَ قَوْمًا مَّا أَتَنهُم مِّن نَذِيرٍ مِّن قَبلِكَ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَتَذَكَرُونَ وَلَوْلَا أَن تُصِيبَهُم مُّصِيبَةٌ بِمَا قَدَّمَتْ أَيْدِيهِمْ

والمقصود أن متأخريهم الذين هم أعلم منهم جعلوا عِلمَ الرب يحصل بواسطة القياس البرهاني»، وكذلك علم أنبيائه وقد بسطنا الكلام في الردّ [١٤٤] عليهم في
غير هذا الموضع . والمقصود هنا التنبيه على فساد قولهم : إنه لا يحصل العلم إلا بالبرهان الذي وصفوه. وإذا كان هذا السلب باطلاً في علم آحاد الناس كان بطلانه أولى في علم رب العالمين سبحانه وتعالى، ثم ملائكته وأنبيائه - صلوات الله عليهم أجمعين .
انتهى الوجه السادس من المقام الثالث
The third station - The sixth aspect: The statement that Allah's knowledge also occurs through syllogism. ... And the purpose is that their later ones, who are more knowledgeable among them, made the knowledge of the Lord occur through the demonstrative syllogism, and likewise the knowledge of His prophets. And we have expanded the speech in refutation of them elsewhere. And the purpose here is to draw attention to the corruption of their statement that knowledge is not obtained except through the demonstration they described. And if this negation is false in the knowledge of individual people, then its falsehood is even more so in the knowledge of the Lord of the worlds, glorified and exalted, then His angels and His prophets - peace be upon them all. End of the sixth aspect of the third station.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that knowledge is only obtained through the demonstrative syllogism (burhan) they have defined. He begins by linking this claim to arrogance and following Pharaoh, citing Qur'anic verses about those who argue against Allah's signs without authority. The verses from Surah Ghafir and Surah Qasas illustrate the fate of the arrogant who reject the clear signs brought by messengers. Ibn Taymiyya then states that the later logicians extended this claim to Allah's own knowledge and the knowledge of prophets, asserting that even divine knowledge is acquired via qiyas burhani. He argues that this is false: if the claim that knowledge is only through burhan is invalid for ordinary humans (since they acquire knowledge through other means like senses, revelation, and fitra), then it is even more unfounded for Allah, whose knowledge is perfect and independent. The structure follows a pattern: citation of Qur'anic examples of arrogant opposition, then direct refutation. The key logical move is the argument a fortiori (from the lesser to the greater): if burhan is not necessary for human knowledge, it certainly is not for divine knowledge. This passage exemplifies Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the epistemological monopoly of Greek logic.

Key Terms
القياس البرهاني
البرهان
العلم

C) Because Allah only uses revelation, not syllogism.
D) Because humans cannot understand Allah's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim by showing that if it fails for humans, it certainly fails for Allah, using a clear a fortiori argument.

The Conclusion as a Single Relational Judgment
Ibn Taymiyya redefines the conclusion as a single relational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's concept of the conclusion as a single relational judgment (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) and contrast it with the logicians' view of two parts.

Core Idea
The conclusion is one relational fact, not two parts combined.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' standard description of a conclusion as having two distinct parts (subject and predicate). He argues that this is only true in verbal expression; in the intellect, the conclusion is a single relational judgment: the affirmation or negation of a relation between the subject and predicate. Understanding this shift is key to his broader critique of syllogistic formalism.

Guided Path
1
Recall the logicians' view
Logicians claim that the conclusion of a syllogism has two parts: a subject and a predicate, which are combined.
2
Identify the verbal vs. intellectual distinction
Ibn Taymiyya says that in verbal expression (fi al-natiq bihi) it appears as two parts, but in the intellect (fi al-‘aql) the desired meaning is a single relational fact.
He uses terms like 'thubut al-nisba al-hukmiyya' or 'intifa'uha'.
3
Understand the relational judgment
The relational judgment (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) is the affirmation or negation of a property to a thing. For example, in 'nabidh is forbidden', the desired conclusion is the affirmation of the prohibition for nabidh—one thing.
This is the single meaning intended by the proposition.
4
Apply to examples
Consider 'the human is sensitive'. The conclusion is the affirmation of sensation for the human—one relational fact, not two separate elements.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes this unity to question the need for two premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوت النسبة فيقال لهم : أما المطلوب الذي لا يزيد على جزئين فذاك في النطق به. والمطلوب أو انتفاؤها في العقل إنما هو شيء واحد، لا اثنان . وهو ثبوت النسبة الحكمية أو انتفاؤها؛ وإن شئت قلت: «اتصاف الموصوف بالصفة نفياً أو إثباتاً»؛ وإن شئت قلت : «نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع والخبر إلى المبتدأ نفياً أو إثباتاً»؛ وأمثال ذلك من العبارات الدالة على المعنى الواحد المقصود بـ «القضية». فإذا كان النتيجة أن النبيذ حرام أو ليس بحرام»، أو «الإنسان حساس أو ليس بحساس، أو نحو ذلك، فالمطلوب ثبوت التحريم للنبيذ أو انتفاؤه، وكذلك ثبوت الحس للإنسان أو انتفاؤه والمقدمة الواحدة إذا ناسبت ذلك المطلوب حصل بها واحدة المقصود. وقولنا : النبيذ خمر يناسب المطلوب، وكذلك قولنا : الإنسان حيوان». فإذا كان الإنسان يعلم أن كل خمر حرام، ولكن يشك في النبيذ المتنازع فيه هل يسمى في لغة الشارع «خمراً»، فقيل : النبيذ حرام لأنه قد ثبت في الصحيح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : كل مسكر خمر، كانت هذه القضية، وهي قولنا : «قد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : (إن) كل مسكر خمر، يفيد تحريم النبيذ، وإن كان نفس قوله قد تضمن قضية أخرى.
لا لزوم لذكر والاستدلال بذلك مشروط بتقديم مقدمات معلومة عند المستمع، وهي إنَّ ما أهلُ العلم بالحديث فقد وجب التصديق بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم المقدمات قاله» و «إن ما حرمه الرسولُ فهو حرام»، ونحو ذلك.
فلو لزم أن يُذكر كلُّ ما يتوقف عليه العلم وإن كان معلوماً كانت المقدمات أكثر من اثنتين، بل قد تكون أكثر من عشر . وعلى ما قالوه فينبغي لكل من استدل بقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يقول : النبي حرم ذلك، وما حرمه فهو حرام، فهذا حرام». وكذلك يقول : النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أوجب، وما أوجبه النبي فقد وجب، فهذا قد وجب». وإذا احتج على تحريم الأمهات والبنات ونحو ذلك يحتاج أن يقول : إن الله حرم
The affirmation of the relation. So it is said to them: As for the desired conclusion that does not exceed two parts, that is in its verbal expression. But the desired conclusion or its negation in the intellect is only one thing, not two. It is the affirmation or negation of the relational judgment. If you wish, say: 'The attribution of the attribute to the described, negatively or affirmatively'; if you wish, say: 'The relation of the predicate to the subject and the predicate to the subject, negatively or affirmatively'; and similar expressions indicating the single meaning intended by 'proposition'. So if the result is that nabidh is forbidden or not forbidden, or 'the human is sensitive or not sensitive', or the like, then the desired thing is the affirmation of prohibition for nabidh or its negation, and likewise the affirmation of sensation for the human or its negation. The single premise, if it is appropriate for that desired thing, achieves the intended thing singly. And our saying: 'Nabidh is wine' is appropriate for the desired thing, and likewise our saying: 'The human is an animal'. So if a person knows that every wine is forbidden, but is in doubt concerning the disputed nabidh whether it is called 'wine' in the language of the Lawgiver, and it is said: Nabidh is forbidden because it has been established in the Sahih from the Prophet (PBUH) that he said: 'Every intoxicant is wine', then this proposition, which is our saying: 'The Prophet (PBUH) said: Every intoxicant is wine', yields the prohibition of nabidh, even though his very statement included another proposition.
There is no necessity to mention... And reasoning by that is conditional upon presenting premises known to the listener, namely that whoever is a scholar of hadith must believe that the Prophet said it, and that 'Whatever the Messenger forbade is forbidden', and the like.
For if it were necessary to mention everything on which knowledge depends, even if known, then the premises would be more than two, indeed they could be more than ten. And according to what they say, it would be incumbent on everyone who reasons by a saying of the Prophet (PBUH) to say: 'The Prophet forbade that, and whatever the Prophet forbade is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' And likewise he says: 'The Prophet (PBUH) made it obligatory, and what the Prophet made obligatory has become obligatory, so this is obligatory.' And if he argues for the prohibition of mothers and daughters and the like, he needs to say: 'Verily Allah has forbidden...'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critical refutation of the logicians' claim that a syllogism requires exactly two premises. He begins by clarifying the nature of the desired conclusion (matlub). The logicians say the conclusion has two parts (subject and predicate), but Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is only in verbal expression (fi al-natiq bihi). In the intellect (fi al-‘aql), the desired conclusion is a single thing: the affirmation or negation of a relational judgment (thubut al-nisba al-hukmiyya aw intifauha). He explains this with phrases like 'attribution of the attribute to the described' or 'relation of predicate to subject', all indicating the same single meaning intended by a proposition (qadiyya). For example, the result that nabidh is forbidden is one thing: the affirmation of prohibition for nabidh. A single premise that matches that desired thing can achieve it, e.g., 'nabidh is wine' if we know that all wine is forbidden. But if there is doubt about whether nabidh is called 'wine' in the Lawgiver's language, we might need a premise like 'every intoxicant is wine' from the Prophet's saying. That one premise (the hadith) itself contains another proposition (that every intoxicant is wine) but is sufficient to yield the conclusion that nabidh is forbidden, given that the listener already knows that the Prophet's statement is authoritative and that whatever He forbade is forbidden. The reasoning (istidlal) is conditional on these known premises (muqaddimat ma‘luma). Ibn Taymiyya then points out that if we were required to state every such known premise, the number of premises would exceed two, possibly more than ten. For instance, reasoning from a hadith would require stating that the Prophet said it, that his prohibition is binding, etc. Similarly, reasoning from the Quran would require mentioning that Allah forbade certain relatives. Thus, the logicians' insistence on exactly two premises is arbitrary and impractical. Ibn Taymiyya uses Islamic legal examples to illustrate: the prohibition of nabidh via the hadith 'every intoxicant is wine', the obligation of acts based on prophetic command, and the prohibition of mothers and daughters based on Quranic text. This passage demonstrates his broader critique of the Aristotelian syllogistic framework when applied to actual reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
النسبة الحكمية
المطلوب
مقدمة واحدة

Key Takeaway
The conclusion is a single relational fact, not a composite of two terms; this reframes how we understand syllogistic reasoning.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Two-Premise Requirement
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the two-premise requirement using a hadith example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that a single premise can suffice for a conclusion given known background premises, and that requiring all premises explicitly leads to more than two premises.

Core Idea
One appropriate premise plus known background can yield the conclusion; the two-premise rule is arbitrary.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya extends his critique to the syllogistic requirement of exactly two premises. Using the example of deriving the prohibition of nabidh from the hadith 'every intoxicant is wine', he shows that a single premise (the hadith) yields the conclusion because the listener already knows that the Prophet's words are authoritative and that his prohibitions are binding. If we had to state all those known premises, the number would exceed two, even reaching more than ten. This challenges the Aristotelian ideal of a streamlined syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard syllogistic requirement
Logicians argue that every valid syllogism must have exactly two premises (e.g., major and minor) to produce a conclusion.
2
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's example
Consider the reasoning: 'Nabidh is forbidden because the Prophet said: every intoxicant is wine, and we know that every wine is forbidden.' Ibn Taymiyya points out that the single premise 'the Prophet said: every intoxicant is wine' suffices, given the listener's known background.
Background includes: the authority of the Prophet, the binding nature of his prohibitions, etc.
3
Identify the implicit premises
If we were to make all background explicit, we would need to state that the Prophet said it, that his words are true, that his prohibitions are binding, etc. This would require many premises, not just two.
Ibn Taymiyya says they could be more than ten.
4
Generalize the critique
The requirement of exactly two premises is arbitrary and impractical for actual reasoning in Islamic sciences, where reasoning often relies on a body of known principles.
This applies to reasoning from Quran and Sunnah.

ثبوت النسبة فيقال لهم : أما المطلوب الذي لا يزيد على جزئين فذاك في النطق به. والمطلوب أو انتفاؤها في العقل إنما هو شيء واحد، لا اثنان . وهو ثبوت النسبة الحكمية أو انتفاؤها؛ وإن شئت قلت: «اتصاف الموصوف بالصفة نفياً أو إثباتاً»؛ وإن شئت قلت : «نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع والخبر إلى المبتدأ نفياً أو إثباتاً»؛ وأمثال ذلك من العبارات الدالة على المعنى الواحد المقصود بـ «القضية». فإذا كان النتيجة أن النبيذ حرام أو ليس بحرام»، أو «الإنسان حساس أو ليس بحساس، أو نحو ذلك، فالمطلوب ثبوت التحريم للنبيذ أو انتفاؤه، وكذلك ثبوت الحس للإنسان أو انتفاؤه والمقدمة الواحدة إذا ناسبت ذلك المطلوب حصل بها واحدة المقصود. وقولنا : النبيذ خمر يناسب المطلوب، وكذلك قولنا : الإنسان حيوان». فإذا كان الإنسان يعلم أن كل خمر حرام، ولكن يشك في النبيذ المتنازع فيه هل يسمى في لغة الشارع «خمراً»، فقيل : النبيذ حرام لأنه قد ثبت في الصحيح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : كل مسكر خمر، كانت هذه القضية، وهي قولنا : «قد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : (إن) كل مسكر خمر، يفيد تحريم النبيذ، وإن كان نفس قوله قد تضمن قضية أخرى.
لا لزوم لذكر والاستدلال بذلك مشروط بتقديم مقدمات معلومة عند المستمع، وهي إنَّ ما أهلُ العلم بالحديث فقد وجب التصديق بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم المقدمات قاله» و «إن ما حرمه الرسولُ فهو حرام»، ونحو ذلك.
فلو لزم أن يُذكر كلُّ ما يتوقف عليه العلم وإن كان معلوماً كانت المقدمات أكثر من اثنتين، بل قد تكون أكثر من عشر . وعلى ما قالوه فينبغي لكل من استدل بقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يقول : النبي حرم ذلك، وما حرمه فهو حرام، فهذا حرام». وكذلك يقول : النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أوجب، وما أوجبه النبي فقد وجب، فهذا قد وجب». وإذا احتج على تحريم الأمهات والبنات ونحو ذلك يحتاج أن يقول : إن الله حرم
The affirmation of the relation. So it is said to them: As for the desired conclusion that does not exceed two parts, that is in its verbal expression. But the desired conclusion or its negation in the intellect is only one thing, not two. It is the affirmation or negation of the relational judgment. If you wish, say: 'The attribution of the attribute to the described, negatively or affirmatively'; if you wish, say: 'The relation of the predicate to the subject and the predicate to the subject, negatively or affirmatively'; and similar expressions indicating the single meaning intended by 'proposition'. So if the result is that nabidh is forbidden or not forbidden, or 'the human is sensitive or not sensitive', or the like, then the desired thing is the affirmation of prohibition for nabidh or its negation, and likewise the affirmation of sensation for the human or its negation. The single premise, if it is appropriate for that desired thing, achieves the intended thing singly. And our saying: 'Nabidh is wine' is appropriate for the desired thing, and likewise our saying: 'The human is an animal'. So if a person knows that every wine is forbidden, but is in doubt concerning the disputed nabidh whether it is called 'wine' in the language of the Lawgiver, and it is said: Nabidh is forbidden because it has been established in the Sahih from the Prophet (PBUH) that he said: 'Every intoxicant is wine', then this proposition, which is our saying: 'The Prophet (PBUH) said: Every intoxicant is wine', yields the prohibition of nabidh, even though his very statement included another proposition.
There is no necessity to mention... And reasoning by that is conditional upon presenting premises known to the listener, namely that whoever is a scholar of hadith must believe that the Prophet said it, and that 'Whatever the Messenger forbade is forbidden', and the like.
For if it were necessary to mention everything on which knowledge depends, even if known, then the premises would be more than two, indeed they could be more than ten. And according to what they say, it would be incumbent on everyone who reasons by a saying of the Prophet (PBUH) to say: 'The Prophet forbade that, and whatever the Prophet forbade is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' And likewise he says: 'The Prophet (PBUH) made it obligatory, and what the Prophet made obligatory has become obligatory, so this is obligatory.' And if he argues for the prohibition of mothers and daughters and the like, he needs to say: 'Verily Allah has forbidden...'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critical refutation of the logicians' claim that a syllogism requires exactly two premises. He begins by clarifying the nature of the desired conclusion (matlub). The logicians say the conclusion has two parts (subject and predicate), but Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is only in verbal expression (fi al-natiq bihi). In the intellect (fi al-‘aql), the desired conclusion is a single thing: the affirmation or negation of a relational judgment (thubut al-nisba al-hukmiyya aw intifauha). He explains this with phrases like 'attribution of the attribute to the described' or 'relation of predicate to subject', all indicating the same single meaning intended by a proposition (qadiyya). For example, the result that nabidh is forbidden is one thing: the affirmation of prohibition for nabidh. A single premise that matches that desired thing can achieve it, e.g., 'nabidh is wine' if we know that all wine is forbidden. But if there is doubt about whether nabidh is called 'wine' in the Lawgiver's language, we might need a premise like 'every intoxicant is wine' from the Prophet's saying. That one premise (the hadith) itself contains another proposition (that every intoxicant is wine) but is sufficient to yield the conclusion that nabidh is forbidden, given that the listener already knows that the Prophet's statement is authoritative and that whatever He forbade is forbidden. The reasoning (istidlal) is conditional on these known premises (muqaddimat ma‘luma). Ibn Taymiyya then points out that if we were required to state every such known premise, the number of premises would exceed two, possibly more than ten. For instance, reasoning from a hadith would require stating that the Prophet said it, that his prohibition is binding, etc. Similarly, reasoning from the Quran would require mentioning that Allah forbade certain relatives. Thus, the logicians' insistence on exactly two premises is arbitrary and impractical. Ibn Taymiyya uses Islamic legal examples to illustrate: the prohibition of nabidh via the hadith 'every intoxicant is wine', the obligation of acts based on prophetic command, and the prohibition of mothers and daughters based on Quranic text. This passage demonstrates his broader critique of the Aristotelian syllogistic framework when applied to actual reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
النسبة الحكمية
المطلوب
مقدمة واحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadith itself says that wine is forbidden.
B) Because the listener already knows that the Prophet's words are authoritative and that his prohibitions are binding.
C) Because nabidh is not wine, so the premise is unnecessary.
D) Because the Quran explicitly forbids nabidh.

Key Takeaway
A single premise can yield a conclusion when it is appropriate and the listener already knows the necessary background premises; the strict two-premise rule is not universally applicable.

The Four Claims of the Logicians and Their Falsehood
Ibn Taymiyya lists the four claims of the logicians and declares three of them false outright.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four claims the logicians make about their science and explain Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the first three.

Core Idea
The logicians claim their method is the only path to knowledge; Ibn Taymiyya shows this is false.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya addresses the logicians' assertion that their discipline is a communal obligation (fard kifaya). He identifies four central claims that underpin this assertion: two negative (conceptions and assents cannot be attained without their method) and two positive (their method yields new conceptions and certain demonstration). Ibn Taymiyya declares the first three claims obviously false and sets the stage for a detailed refutation of the fourth. By examining these claims, you will learn how Ibn Taymiyya systematically critiques the epistemological foundations of Aristotelian logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' analogy
Find the analogy the logicians use to describe their logic's relationship to meanings. How does Ibn Taymiyya report their simile?
The analogy is: logic is to meanings as prosody to poetry, scales to goods, cubit to measured objects.
2
Extract the four claims
Ibn Taymiyya enumerates four specific claims: two negative and two positive. Write them down in your own words. The passage explicitly states them.
Negative: conceptions not attained except by their method; assents not attained except by their method. Positive: their method obtains new conceptions; their demonstration yields certainty.
3
Distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's verdict on each
For each claim, determine whether Ibn Taymiyya accepts or rejects it. He says the first three are false; the fourth is 'better' but will be discussed later.
He calls the two negative claims 'among the most obviously false'. The positive claim about new conceptions is also 'bāṭil'. The fourth is 'amthal' (better) but not accepted.
4
Analyze the grounds of refutation
Ibn Taymiyya gives reasons: logic does not distinguish proof from non-proof, it contains falsehoods that corrupt sciences, and the claim of protecting the mind is 'among the most false claims'. Identify these grounds.
He argues that if logic had truth, it would be like any author's speech; but it also contains falsehoods. The protective claim is false.

The third station - The invalidity of the statement that learning logic is a communal obligation. And they claim that the relationship of their logic to meanings is like the relationship of prosody to poetry, and the scales of wealth to wealth, and the scales of times to times, and like the relationship of the cubit to measured things. And this is what the majority of Muslim scholars and other rational people said: it is false. For their logic does not distinguish between proof and non-proof - neither in the form of proof nor in its matter. Nor is there a need to weigh meanings with it; rather, it is not valid to weigh meanings with it as it is. And if there is truth in it, then there must be truth in the speech of every author. Rather, it contains false things that, if sciences are weighed with them, they would corrupt them. And their claim that it is a legal instrument whose observance protects the mind from slipping in its thought is a false claim, indeed one of the most false claims. The discussion with them is only about the meanings they placed in logic and claimed that 'required conceptions are only attained through them' and 'required assents are only attained through them.' So they mentioned for their logic four claims - two negative and two positive. They claimed that 'conceptions are not attained except by the method they mentioned therein' and 'assents are not attained except by the method they mentioned therein.' These two claims are among the most obviously false claims. And they claimed that 'the method they mentioned obtains the conception of realities that were not conceived' - this is also false. And the evidence on these three claims has preceded, and discussion will come on their fourth claim, which is better than the others, which is their claim that their demonstration yields
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya, specifically addressing the claim that learning logic is a communal obligation (fard kifaya). He begins by quoting the logicians' analogy: logic is to meanings as prosody is to poetry, or as measuring scales are to goods, or a cubit to measured objects. Ibn Taymiyya reports that the majority of Muslim scholars and rational people say this is false. The reason is that logic does not actually distinguish between valid and invalid proofs, either in form or content. Moreover, meanings do not need to be weighed by logic, and even if there is some truth in it, it contains falsehoods that corrupt the sciences. The claim that logic is an 'instrument that protects the mind from error' is extremely false. Then Ibn Taymiyya identifies the core issue: the logicians claim that required conceptions and assents are only attained through their method. He enumerates four claims: two negative (conceptions not attained without their method; assents not attained without their method) and two positive (their method yields conception of previously unknown realities; their demonstration yields certainty). He declares the first three claims obviously false, and says the fourth is the best of them but not accepted. This sets up the structure of his broader critique.

آلة قانونية (ala qanuniyya)
تصورات (tasawwurat)
تصديقات (tasdiqat)
برهان (burhan)

C) The analogy between logic and measuring tools.
D) The claim that logic protects the mind from error.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya identifies four claims of the logicians, rejects three as false, and sets the stage for a critique of the fourth, exposing the arrogance of claiming an exclusive path to knowledge.

Why Ibn Taymiyya Rejects the Two-Premise Rule
Ibn Taymiyya argues that only Greek logicians insist on exactly two premises in reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the logicians' two-premise requirement and identify his alternative method.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya argues that restricting inference to two premises is a Greek innovation, not a universal rational norm.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a core tenet of Aristotelian logic: that all reasoning must consist of exactly two premises forming a syllogism. In this passage, he argues that this requirement is unique to Greek logicians and their followers, and that it contradicts the practice of rational people across disciplines, especially Muslim theorists. Let's walk through his critique to see how he defends flexibility in inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
First, locate where Ibn Taymiyya states what the logicians insist on: that inference must be by exactly two premises, no more and no less.
The key phrase is 'لا يلتزم الاستدلال بمقدمتين فقط إلا أهل المنطق'
2
Find the basis of Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that this requirement is not found among other rational groups: Muslims, grammarians, physicians, etc. Find the list of groups he mentions who do not adhere to the two-premise rule.
Look for 'المهاجرين والأنصار والتابعين لهم بإحسان' and 'أهل النحو والطب والهندسة'
3
Understand his alternative method
Ibn Taymiyya states that inference may require one, two, or many premises, and Muslim theorists use what is needed. Identify his description of the Muslim method.
The phrase 'الاستدلال تارة يقف على مقدمة وتارة على مقدمتين وتارة على مقدمات' is crucial.
4
Note the consequences of blind imitation
He criticizes those who imitate the logicians, saying it leads to useless or harmful knowledge. Find the specific criticisms.
Look for 'ما استفادوا بما تلقوه عنهم علماً إلا علماً يستغني عن باطل كلامهم'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يلتزم الاستدلال بمقدمتين فقط إلا أهل المنطق ولهذا لا تجد في سائر طوائف العقلاء ومصنفي العلوم مَنْ يلتزم في استدلاله البيانَ بمقدمتين لا أكثر ولا أقل، ويجتهد في رد الزيادة إلى ثنتين وفي تكميل النقص بجعله مقدمتين، إلا أهلُ منطق اليونان ومن سَلَك سبيلهم ، دون من كان باقياً على فطرته السليمة، أو سلك مسلك غيرهم كالمهاجرين والأنصار والتابعين لهم بإحسان، وسائر أئمة المسلمين، وعلمائهم، ونظارهم، وسائر طوائف الملل. وكذلك أهل النحو والطب والهندسة، لا يدخل في هذا الباب إلا من أتبع في ذلك هؤلاء المنطقيين، كما قلدوهم في الحدود المركبة من «الجنس» و «الفصل». وما استفادوا بما تلقوه عنهم علماً إلا علماً يستغني عن باطل كلامهم، أو ما يضر ولا ينفع لما فيه من الجهل أو التطويل الكثير . ولهذا لما كان الاستدلالُ تارة يقف على مقدمة وتارة على مقدمتين، وتارة على مقدمات، كانت طريقةُ نُظّار المسلمين أن يذكروا من الأدلة على المقدمات ما يحتاجون إليه ؛ ولا يلتزمون في كل استدلال أن يذكروا مقدمتين كما يفعله من يسلك سبيل المنطقيين. بل كُتُب نظار المسلمين وخطابهم وسلوكهم في نظرهم لأنفسهم ومناظرتهم لغيرهم تعليماً، وإرشاداً ومجادلة على ما ذكرتُ. وكذلك سائر أصناف العقلاء من أهل الملل ، وغيرهم إلا من سلك طريق هؤلاء. وما زال نظار المسلمين يَعِيبون طريقة أهل المنطق، ويبينون ما فيها من العِيِّ، واللُّكْنة، وقصور العقل، وعجز النطق ويبينون أنها إلى إفساد المنطق العقلي واللساني أقرب منها إلى تقويم ذلك. ولا يرضون أن يسلكوها في نظرهم ومناظرتهم.
Only the logicians adhere to inference by exactly two premises. For this reason, you do not find among the other classes of rational people and authors of sciences anyone who, in his inference, commits to explaining by two premises, no more and no less, and strives to reduce excess to two and to complete a deficiency by making it two premises, except the Greek logicians and those who follow their path, excluding those who remain upon their sound natural disposition, or who follow the path of others like the Emigrants, the Helpers, and those who followed them with excellence, and the rest of the Muslim imams, scholars, and theorists, and all classes of religious communities. Likewise the grammarians, physicians, and geometers do not enter into this matter except those who follow these logicians in that, as they imitated them in definitions composed of 'genus' and 'differentia'. And what they gained from what they received from them is only knowledge that can dispense with the falsehood of their speech, or something harmful and useless because of the ignorance or extreme prolixity in it. And for this reason, since inference sometimes stops at one premise, sometimes at two, and sometimes at many, the method of the Muslim theorists is to mention from the evidences for the premises what they need; they do not commit to mentioning only two premises in every inference, as does the one who follows the path of the logicians. Rather, the books of the Muslim theorists, their speech, and their practice in their own theorizing and in their disputation with others—teaching, guiding, and arguing—are as I have described. And so too are all the classes of rational people among the religious communities and others, except those who follow the path of these. And the Muslim theorists have never ceased criticizing the method of the logicians, showing what is in it of stammering, halting, deficiency of intellect, and incapacity of expression, and showing that it is closer to corrupting rational and linguistic logic than to straightening it. And they are not content to follow it in their theorizing and disputation.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' requirement that all reasoning must consist of exactly two premises (a syllogism). Ibn Taymiyya asserts that only the Greek logicians and their followers adhere to this stricture. He observes that no other rational people—whether scholars of various sciences or ordinary people of sound nature—impose such a limit. He specifically exempts the early Muslims (Muhajirun, Ansar, and their followers) and the imams and theorists of Islam. Even specialists in grammar, medicine, and geometry do not adopt this method except those who blindly imitate the logicians, and such imitation yields only useless or harmful knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya then presents the alternative Muslim method: inference may require one premise, two, or many, and Muslim theorists (mutakallimun) use what is needed without artificial restriction. He concludes by noting that Muslim theorists have always criticized the logicians' method, pointing out its flaws—stammering, halting, intellectual deficiency, and expressive incapacity—and they refuse to use it in their own theorizing and debates.

Key Terms
المقدمة
الاستدلال
المنطقيون

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the logicians' two-premise requirement as a Greek innovation, advocating instead for flexible inference based on the needs of the argument.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Philosophers' Intellect Doctrine
Ibn Taymiyya declares the philosophers' intellect doctrine as blasphemous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of the philosophers' intellect doctrine that Ibn Taymiyya condemns and explain why he considers it kufr.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the philosophers' view that created intellects can originate the world, as that usurps God's sole creative power.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya delivers a sharp verdict on the philosophers' teaching about the intellect. He does not mince words: what they call 'aql is not merely mistaken but constitutes the worst form of disbelief. To understand why, we must look at what the philosophers actually claim—that the First Intellect creates everything other than God, and the Tenth Intellect creates the sublunar world. For Ibn Taymiyya, this is a direct affront to tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Locate what the philosophers affirm about 'aql according to Ibn Taymiyya. Look for the description of the First Intellect and the Tenth Intellect.
2
Understand the claim
The First Intellect is said to be 'mubdi' kulla ma siwa Allah' — originator of everything other than God. The Tenth Intellect originates the sublunar world. Note the word 'mubdi'' which implies creation ex nihilo, a divine prerogative.
3
Recognize the implications
If true, this would mean that created intellects are co-creators with God. This directly contradicts the Islamic doctrine that Allah alone is the Creator (al-Khaliq).
4
Grasp the severity of the charge
Ibn Taymiyya calls this 'min a'zam al-kufr' — among the greatest disbelief. He extends this judgement to Muslims, Jews, and Christians, indicating that it violates the core monotheistic belief in God's exclusive creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a blunt critique. Ibn Taymiyya opens by stating that the philosophers' concept of 'al-'aql' (the intellect) is false and constitutes major disbelief (kufr). He specifies two key intellects in their hierarchy: the First Intellect, which they claim creates everything else except God, and the Tenth Intellect, which they claim creates the sublunar world. This, he asserts, is rejected by all monotheists.

Understanding Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Logicians' Claim on Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that only logicians' demonstration yields knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's core objection to the logicians' claim that theoretical knowledge requires their demonstration, and evaluate its logical basis.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the monopoly on certainty, not syllogistic reasoning.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's rebuttal of the logicians focuses on their crucial error: not what they affirmed, but what they denied. He clarifies that his target is their exclusivist claim that certainty comes only through their form of demonstration. Let's dissect his argument and see how it opens the door for other valid methods of reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Find the exact phrase where Ibn Taymiyya states the logicians' claim: that theoretical knowledge (al-'ilm al-nazari) is not obtained except through their demonstration (burhan). This is the target of his critique.
The claim is at the end of the passage.
2
Distinguish between error in affirmation and error in denial
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says his purpose is not to clarify what they erred in affirming, but what they erred in denying. Understand that he accepts some truths in their logic (e.g., the first figure) but rejects their denial of other valid reasoning.
This distinction is key to understanding the scope of his critique.
3
Analyze the role of the first figure syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya mentions the first figure as a case where logicians 'lengthened the path' and made simple truths complicated. Note that he does not deny its validity, only that it requires their elaborate method.
He implies that sound intellects can grasp such truths without formal training.
4
Evaluate the basis of Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Assess the epistemological grounds: Ibn Taymiyya appeals to sound intellects, prophetic knowledge, and the consensus of rational people. This suggests his critique is not merely polemical but rooted in a broader theory of knowledge.
Consider how this aligns with Usul al-Fiqh's recognition of multiple sources of certainty.

(۲) على هامش الأصل: قد ذكر ابن خلكان أن ابن سينا تاب قبيل ،موته وأعتق مماليكه، وأقبل على تلاوة القرآن» .

الشكل الأول واتفق أن فيه أموراً ظاهرة مثل الشكل الأول ، ولا يعرفون أن ما فيه من الحق لا يعرف بدون يحتاج إليهم فيه، بل طولوا فيه الطريق، وسلكوا الوَعِر والضيق، ولم يهتدوا فيه إلى ما يفيد المنطق التحقيق.
وليس المقصود في هذا المقام بيان ما أخطأوا في إثباته، بل ما أخطأوا في نفيه، حيث زعموا أن العلم النظري لا يحصل إلا ببرهانهم»، وهو من «القياس».
The confusion of the matter of logic upon a group who did not conceive its realities. They are most of [185] what they [the logicians] spend on those who do not understand what they say and magnify them by ignorance and illusion, or understand some of what they say, or most of it, or all of it, while not conceiving in that state the reality of what the Messenger brought, may God bless him and grant him peace, and what is known by sound intellects, and what the rest of the rational people said, contradictory to what they said. And he [the follower] only reached the end of their matter after toil and hardship, and good opinion accompanied it, so he became entangled in their misguidance in a way only God knows. Then if God takes care of him after that – as happened to many excellent people who initially thought well of them, then their misguidance became clear to them, necessitating their turning away from them, and disavowing them, rather, refuting them – otherwise he remains in misguidance.
