(2) In the margin of the original: Ibn Khallikan mentioned that Ibn Sina repented before his death, freed his slaves, and turned to reciting the Qur'an. And their misguidance in divine matters is apparent to most people, and for this reason all the Muslim theoreticians declared them disbelievers in that respect. Rather, logic became confusing for a group who did not conceive its realities and implications, and did not know what the rest of the rational people said concerning their contradictions in it. The first figure – and it happens that there are apparent matters in it like the first figure, and they do not know that what is true in it is not known without needing them in it. Rather, they lengthened the path in it, and traversed rough and narrow terrain, and did not attain in it what would benefit logic of verification. And the purpose in this place is not to clarify what they erred in affirming, but what they erred in denying, when they claimed that theoretical knowledge is not obtained except through their 'demonstration', which is from 'syllogism'.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition. Ibn Taymiyya describes the confusion of a group who did not grasp the realities of logic. He explains that logicians often target those who do not understand their speech, and these followers, due to ignorance and false trust, become entangled in misguidance after much difficulty. Some later saw the truth and recanted, like Ibn Sina's reported repentance. Then he focuses on the logicians' error in denying the validity of other forms of reasoning. He mentions the first figure of syllogism, noting that logicians make it seem complicated but actually contain simple truths that do not require their elaborate methods. The key point: Ibn Taymiyya is not criticizing what logicians assert (e.g., the first figure), but what they deny – that theoretical knowledge can only come through their specific demonstration (burhan) which is a type of syllogism (qiyas). He thus lays the groundwork for his later argument that analogical reasoning (tamthil) can also yield certainty.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' error was not in what they affirmed but in what they denied—that theoretical knowledge can only come from their demonstration, thus excluding other valid forms of reasoning recognized in Islamic sciences.

The Primacy of Material Content over Syllogistic Form in Inference
Argues inference depends on material content, not syllogistic form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' restriction of demonstration to certain premisses, and evaluate his argument that inference depends on material content rather than syllogistic form.

Core Idea
Inference is driven by real relations, not formal shapes.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the core claim of formal logic: that valid inference depends primarily on the structure of the syllogism. He argues that all reasoning, whether in theology, law, or daily life, relies on knowledge of things and their necessary concomitants—relations that the human fitra grasps directly. In this passage, he attacks the logicians' exclusion of estimative and well-known propositions from demonstration, showing that this exclusion undermines the very sciences they claim to serve.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's starting point
He asserts that everything has a necessary concomitant (lazim) and an opposite (munaf). Inference proceeds from affirming the implicans or negating the concomitant. This is the material basis of all reasoning.
2
Understand the fitra's role
The innate faculty (fitra) conceives these relations and can express them in many forms. Ibn Taymiyya stresses that no single form, like the Aristotelian syllogism, is essential.
3
Locate the critique of demonstrative premisses
Find where he criticizes logicians for excluding wahmiyyat (estimative) and mashhoorat (well-known) from demonstration. He claims the fitra's judgment about them is stronger than about many so-called 'certainties'.
4
Follow the consequence
Excluding these premisses, he argues, would cut off the noblest sciences—both theoretical (like kalam) and practical (like fiqh)—because they rely on such propositions. Only mental abstractions remain.
5
Evaluate the polemical aim
The passage ends by restating the purpose: to test logic's claim as an infallible instrument. The critique shows that the instrument fails by its own standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة ما من شيء إلا وله لازم لا يوجد بدونه وله مُنافٍ مضاد لوجوده. فيستدل عليه بثبوت ملزومه وعلى انتفائه بانتفاء لازمه . ويستدل على انتفائه بوجود منافيه ويستدل بانتفاء منافيه على وجوده. وهذا الاستدلال يحصل من العلم بأحوال الشيء، وملزومها، ولوازمها. وإذا تصوّرته الفطرةُ عبرت عنه بأنواع من العبارات وصوّرته في أنواع من صُوَر الأدلة، لا يختص شيء من ذلك بالصورة التي ذكروها في «القياس»، فضلاً عما سموه «البرهان» . فإن البرهان» شَرَطوا له مادّة معينة، وهي القضايا التي ذكروها. وأخرجوا من «الأوليات» ما سموه «وَهْميات» وما سموه مشهورات»، وحكمُ الفطرة بهما ـ لا سيما بما سَمَّوه وهميات» - أعظمُ من حكمها بكثير من اليقينيات» التي جعلوها مواد «البرهان» . وقد بسطت القول على هذا، وبينتُ كلامهم في ذلك وتناقضهم، وأن ما أخرجوه يخرج به ما يُنال به أشرفُ العلوم من العلوم النظرية والعلوم العملية، ولا يبقى بأيديهم إلا أمور مقدَّرة في الأذهان لا حقيقة لها في الأعيان ولولا أن هذا الموضع لا يتسع لحكاية ألفاظهم في هذا ، وما أوردته عليهم، لذكرته . فقد ذكرت ذلك كله في مواضعه من العلوم الكلية والإلهية، فإنها هي المطلوبة. والكلام في المنطق إنما وقع لِمَا زعموه أنه آلة قانونية تعصِمُ مراعاتها الذهن أن يَزِل في فكره. فاحتجنا أن ننظر في هذه الآلة هل هي كما قالوا، أو ليس الأمر كذلك .
And in summary, there is nothing except that it has a necessary concomitant without which it does not exist, and it has an opposite contradictory to its existence. So one infers from the affirmation of its implicans to it, and from the negation of its necessary concomitant to its negation. And one infers from its negation by the existence of its opposite, and from the negation of its opposite to its existence. This inference is obtained from knowledge of the states of the thing, its implicantia, and its necessary concomitants. When the innate faculty (fitra) conceives this, it expresses it with various expressions and depicts it in various forms of proofs; none of that is specific to the form they mentioned in 'syllogism', let alone what they called 'demonstration'. For 'demonstration' they stipulated a specific material, namely the propositions they mentioned. And they excluded from the 'primary propositions' what they called 'wahmiyyat' (estimative) and what they called 'mashhoorat' (well-known); the judgment of the innate faculty about both – especially what they called 'wahmiyyat' – is stronger than its judgment about many of the 'certainties' which they made the materials of 'demonstration'. And I have expanded on this, and clarified their statements about that and their contradiction, and that what they exclude would exclude what is attained of the noblest sciences, both theoretical and practical; nothing remains in their hands except things imagined in minds with no reality in external existence. Were it not that this place cannot accommodate relating their exact words and what I have objected against them, I would mention it. I have indeed mentioned all that in its proper places in the universal and divine sciences, for they are what is sought. The speech about logic only occurred because of their claim that it is a legal instrument which, if observed, protects the mind from slipping in its thought. So we needed to examine this instrument: is it as they said, or is it not so?
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique of the logicians' claim that the syllogistic form, especially in demonstration (burhan), is the sole guarantor of certain knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya argues that all inference is based on material content – the real relations between things, their necessary concomitants and opposites. The human fitra (innate disposition) naturally grasps these relations and can express them in many ways, none of which are confined to the Aristotelian syllogistic form. He specifically attacks the logicians' exclusion of 'wahmiyyat' (estimative propositions) and 'mashhoorat' (well-known opinions) from the material of demonstration. He claims that the fitra's judgment about these is often stronger than about many so-called 'certainties' that they include. Furthermore, he asserts that by restricting demonstration to certain types of premisses, they exclude the most noble sciences (like kalam and fiqh) which rely on such propositions. The result is that their 'demonstration' deals only with mental abstractions with no real existence. He then refers to his fuller treatment elsewhere. Finally, he restates the purpose of his critique: to examine whether logic truly serves as an infallible instrument for thought, as they claim. This passage is typical of Ibn Taymiyya's radical critique of Greek logic's epistemological pretensions, particularly its claim to be the exclusive path to certainty.

Key Terms

ملزوم
مناف
فطرة
وهميات
مشهورات
برهان

B) The material content, i.e., knowledge of things and their real relations.
C) The number of premisses in the argument.
D) The use of demonstrative premisses only.

Key Takeaway
Inference is fundamentally about grasping real relations between things, not about fitting arguments into prescribed forms.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logicians' Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya defines inference based on concomitants and opposites, attacking the logicians' limited definition of demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the logicians' restriction of demonstration and explain why he considers their exclusion of wahmiyyat and mashhurat as harmful to the divine sciences.

Core Idea
Inference is about matter, not form; logicians wrongly exclude reliable premises from demonstration.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya shifts the focus of logic from form to matter. He argues that inference is fundamentally based on our knowledge of a thing's necessary concomitants and opposites, not on the shape of a syllogism. He then attacks the logicians' definition of demonstration (burhān), which requires specific types of certain premises and rejects estimative judgments (wahmiyyāt) and well-known opinions (mashhūrāt). By doing so, he claims they cut off access to the highest sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the exact position Ibn Taymiyya is attacking. He says the logicians stipulated a specific material for 'burhān' and excluded 'wahmiyyāt' and 'mashhūrāt' from the primaries.
This is the logicians' restriction of demonstration to certain premise types.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's alternative
Notice his definition of inference: it pivots on the matter of knowledge, using necessary conditions (malzūm) and opposites (munāfī). He says inference is obtained from knowledge of the states of a thing, its necessary conditions, and its necessary concomitants.
This is a broader, matter-based inference not tied to a specific form.
3
Analyze his grounds
Ibn Taymiyya claims the innate disposition (fiṭra) judges wahmiyyāt and mashhūrāt with greater certainty than many of the logicians' 'certainties'. This is an epistemological objection: he challenges the hierarchy of certainty.
He argues that the excluded premises are often more certain and essential for the noblest sciences.
4
Assess the implications
He concludes that the logicians' restriction leaves only abstract mental constructs with no reality, and excludes the means to attain the divine sciences. He references a fuller treatment elsewhere.
This shows the high stakes: without these premises, theological and practical knowledge becomes inaccessible.

Source Text

The pivot of inference is on the matter of knowledge, not on the form of syllogism. In summary, there is nothing except that it has a necessary concomitant which does not exist without it, and it has an opposite contrary to its existence. So one infers its existence by the existence of its necessary condition, and its non-existence by the non-existence of its necessary concomitant. And one infers its non-existence by the existence of its opposite, and one infers its existence by the non-existence of its opposite, when the matter is confined to the two so that it is impossible for both to be absent, just as it is impossible for both to be present. This inference is obtained from knowledge of the states of a thing, its necessary conditions, and its necessary concomitants. And when the innate disposition conceives it, it expresses it in various types of expressions and pictures it in various types of forms of proofs, none of which is exclusive to the form they mentioned in 'al-Qiyas', let alone what they called 'al-Burhan'. For they stipulated for 'al-Burhan' a specific matter, which are the propositions they mentioned. And they excluded from the 'primaries' what they called 'wahmiyyat' and what they called 'mashhurat', while the judgment of the innate disposition regarding them - especially regarding what they called 'wahmiyyat' - is greater than its judgment regarding many of the 'certainties' which they made the matters of 'al-Burhan'. I have elaborated on this, and shown their statements and their contradiction, and that what they exclude thereby excludes what attains the noblest sciences, both theoretical and practical, and nothing remains in their hands except matters estimated in minds with no reality in external existents. Were it not that this place does not accommodate relating their expressions in this, and what I have advanced against them, I would mention it. I have indeed mentioned all that in its places in the universal and divine sciences, for they are what is sought.

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' conception of inference and demonstration. He begins by stating that inference depends on the matter of knowledge, not on a specific syllogistic form. He describes a general method of inference based on necessary concomitants (lawāzim) and opposites (munāfiyāt). For any thing, there is a necessary condition (malzūm) without which it cannot exist, and a contrary that contradicts its existence. From the existence of the condition, one infers the existence of the thing; from the non-existence of the concomitant, one infers the non-existence of the thing; similarly for opposites. This is a natural, innate method of reasoning. Ibn Taymiyya then criticizes the logicians for restricting demonstration (burhān) to specific types of premises, such as certain primaries (awwaliyyāt), and for excluding what they call 'wahmiyyāt' (estimative judgments) and 'mashhūrāt' (well-known opinions). He argues that the innate disposition (fiṭra) judges these with greater certainty than many of the so-called certainties that logicians accept as material for demonstration. He further claims that by excluding these, the logicians exclude the means to the noblest sciences, leaving only abstract mental constructs with no reality. Ibn Taymiyya references his fuller treatment elsewhere. This critique is foundational to his attack on the Greek logical framework as insufficient for attaining true knowledge, especially in the divine sciences.

Key Terms
استدلال
برهان
وهميات
مشهورات

Ibdā' al-Jāmi' and Ilghā' al-Fāriq: The Middle Term in Qiyas Tamthil
Ibn Taymiyya analyzes the two types of qiyas tamthil and their relation to qiyas shumul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of qiyas tamthil and explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that both contain a middle term equivalent to that in qiyas shumul.

Core Idea
In qiyas tamthil, the common factor or equality is the middle term — just like in qiyas shumul.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' classification of analogical reasoning into two types: presentation of the common factor and elimination of differences. He argues that both are structurally identical to categorical syllogisms, sharing a middle term. This lesson unpacks his analysis and shows how he refutes the claim that analogical reasoning yields only conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of qiyas tamthil
In the Arabic, find ibdā' al-jāmi' and ilghā' al-fāriq. What do these terms mean?
Ibdā' al-jāmi' is presenting the common factor; ilghā' al-fāriq is eliminating the difference.
2
Locate the middle term in each type
For ilghā' al-fāriq, Ibn Taymiyya says equality (musāwāh) is the middle term. For ibdā' al-jāmi', what is the middle term?
The common factor (al-jāmi') itself functions as the middle term.
3
Trace Ibn Taymiyya's rebuttal of the logicians' claim
How does he argue that the certainty/conjecture distinction applies to materials, not to the form itself?
He says that what indicates causality in tamthil is the same as what indicates the truth of the major premise in shumul.
4
Connect to qiyas shumul
Why does Ibn Taymiyya think tamthil and shumul are essentially the same in logical form?
Both rely on a middle term that links the two premises to produce a conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبداء الجامع و «القياس» لا يخلو إما أن يكون بـ «إبداء الجامع» أو بـ «إلغاء الفارق». وإلغاء الفارق و «الجامع» إما العلة وإما دليلها. وأما القياس بإلغاء الفارق فهنا إلغاء الفارق هو أن ما به الحد الأوسط. فإذا قيل : هذا مساو لهذا ومساوي المساوي مساو كانت «المساواة» هي الحد الأوسط، وإلغاء الفارق عبارة عن «المساواة». فإذا قيل: لا فرق بين الفرع والأصل إلا كذا، وهو مُهْدَر، فهو بمنزلة قولك : هذا مساو لهذا، وحكم المساوي حكم مساويه. وأما قولهم : كل ما يدل على أن ما به الاشتراك علة للحكم فظني. فيقال : لا نسلّم، فإن هذه دعوى كلية، ولم تقيموا عليها دليلاً. ثم نقول : الذي يُدَلُّ به على علية المشترك هو الذي يُدَلُّ به على صدق القضية الكبرى وكل ما يُدَلُّ به على صدق الكبرى علة للحكم فظني في قياس الشمول يُدَلُّ به على علية المشترك في قياس التمثيل سواء كان علمياً أو ظنياً.
The presentation of the common [factor] and [qiyas] is either by presenting the common [factor] or by eliminating the difference. Eliminating the difference and the common [factor] are either the cause or its evidence. As for qiyas by eliminating the difference, here eliminating the difference is that what is the middle term. If it is said: This is equal to that, and the equal of the equal is equal, then 'equality' is the middle term, and eliminating the difference is an expression of 'equality'. So if it is said: There is no difference between the branch and the original except such-and-such, and it is disregarded, it is like your saying: This is equal to that, and the ruling of the equal is the ruling of its equal. As for their saying: Everything that indicates that the common description is the cause of the ruling is conjectural. Then it is said: We do not concede, for this is a universal claim, and you have not established a proof for it. Then we say: That by which the causality of the common [description] is indicated is that by which the truth of the major premise is indicated, and everything that indicates the truth of the major premise is the cause of the ruling, so it is conjectural in qiyas shumul [and] that by which the causality of the common [description] is indicated in qiyas tamthil, whether it is certain or conjectural.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' classification of qiyas tamthil (analogical reasoning) into two types: ibdā' al-jāmi' (presenting the common factor) and ilghā' al-fāriq (eliminating the difference). He argues that both types ultimately rely on a middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). In ilghā' al-fāriq, the middle term is equality (musāwāh) between the original and branch, while in ibdā' al-jāmi', it is the common description shared between them. Ibn Taymiyya then critiques the logicians' claim that qiyas tamthil only yields conjecture (zann), whereas qiyas shumul (categorical syllogism) yields certainty. He refutes this by showing that the same process of establishing the causality of the common description is used in both types of qiyas. If the materials are certain, both yield certainty. The reasoning in both is identical: the common description acts as the middle term, the ruling on the original provides the major premise, and applying it to the branch provides the minor premise. Thus, the distinction in epistemic value is not inherent to the form but to the materials used.

Key Terms
الجامع
إلغاء الفارق
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The middle term is a subcategory of the common factor.
D) The common factor is not a middle term.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the common factor or equality serves as the middle term in both types of qiyas tamthil, making them logically equivalent to categorical syllogisms.

Ambiguous Terms and Sophistry in Theological Proofs
Ibn Taymiyya charges the logicians with sophistry due to ambiguous terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ambiguous terms (mujmal) in a theological argument and explain why they render the argument sophistical.

Core Idea
Ambiguous terms in definitions turn any logical proof into sophistry.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his refutation by attacking the very form of the logicians' proof. He charges that their key terms — 'composite', 'need', and 'other' — are all ambiguous (mujmal) and can bear both true and false meanings. Using such ambiguous terms in definitions and syllogisms is, he says, a form of sophistry (safsata). In this lesson we will examine the first step of his critique: the charge of equivocation and why it undermines the entire proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three ambiguous terms
Find the three terms that Ibn Taymiyya explicitly calls 'mujmal' at the start of the passage: 'composite' (murakkab), 'need' (iftiqar), and 'other' (ghayr).
2
List the possible meanings for each term
For each term, note the different meanings Ibn Taymiyya discusses. For example, 'composite' could mean parts assembled externally, or an essence with attributes; 'need' could mean causal dependence or mutual inseparability; 'other' could mean separable or merely distinct.
3
Show how ambiguity destroys the proof
Explain that if the terms are taken in one sense, the premises are false or irrelevant; if taken in another sense, the premises are true but the conclusion does not follow. The proof cannot be evaluated until the terms are fixed.
This is the classic sophistical tactic of equivocation.
4
Conclude why this is sophistry
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that using such ambiguous expressions in definitions and syllogisms is from the category of sophistry (safsata). A proper demonstration (burhan) requires univocal terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - دليل نفاة الصفات والرد عليه
إلى أجزائه، وجزؤه غيره . فـ «المركب مفتقر إلى غيره، والمفتقر إلى غيره ليس واجباً بنفسه .
فيقال : أجزاء هذا الدليل وألفاظه التي تسمونها حدوداً» كلها ألفاظ مجملة تحتمل حقاً وباطلاً واستعمال الألفاظ المجملة في الحدود» و «القياس» من باب السفسطة .
فيقال لكم: قد عُرف أن لفظ «المركب مجمل، وأن المراد به هنا «ذات تقوم بها الافتقار هو صفات». وحينئذ فالمراد بـ «الافتقار» تلازم الذات والصفات» بمعنى أنه لا تلازم الذات توجد الذات إلا مع وجود صفتها المُلازمة لها، ولا توجد الصفة إلا مع وجود الذات الملازمة لها. ولو قدر أنه أريد بـ «التركيب التركيب من الأجزاء الحسية أو العقلية مع تلازم الأجزاء، فهذا معناه .
فإذا قيل : كل مركب مفتقر إلى جزئه، إن عُني به أنه مستلزم لجزئه، وأنه لا وجود يوجد إلا بوجود جزئه، فهذا صحيح. فإن وجود المجموع بدون كلّ من آحاده ممتنع . وإن أريد أنه مفتقر إليه افتقار المفعول إلى فاعله والمعلول إلى علّته الفاعلة، أو إلى القابلة، أو الغائية أو الصُّورية فهذا باطل فإن الواحد من العشرة، والجزء من الجملة : لا يجوز أن يكون فاعلاً، ولا غاية، ولا محلاً لغيره، ولا هو الصورة .
ثم قولكم وجزؤه غيره» ، يقال : لفظ «الغير» يراد به ما كان مبايناً للشيء، وما يطلق عليه ما يجوز مُفارقته له وما ليس إيَّاه. فإن أردتم أنَّ جزء المجموع ما هو مباين له. باطل، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون مبايناً له مع كونه جزءاً منه فيمتنع أن يكون «غيراً» له بهذا الاعتبار. وإن قلتم : يجوز أن يفارقه، فهذا ليس بمسلَّم على الإطلاق، بل يجوز في بعض الأفراد أن يفارق غيره من الأجزاء، ويفارق المجموع الذي هو الهيئة الاجتماعية . ولا يلزم ذلك في كل مجموع ، لا سيما على أصلهم . فإن الفلك» عندهم «مركّب» من أجزائه وصفاته، ولا يجوز عندهم على أجزائه التفرقُ .
والمسلمون وجمهور العقلاء أن الله حي، عليم، قدير؛ ولا يجوز أن يكون صفات الله مفارقه كونه حيّاً عالماً قادراً؛ بل لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك. وكونه حياً، عالماً، قادراً، لوازم ذاته من لوازم ذاته، وهي ملازمة لذاته، لا يجوز عليها الافتراقُ بوجه من الوجوه. فامتنع أن تكون صفاته هذه «أغياراً» بهذا الاعتبار.
وإن فُسّر الغَيْرانِ بما ليس أحدهما هو الآخر، أو بما يجوز العلم بأحدهما مع عدم العلم بالآخر ؛ فلا ريب أن صفة الموصوف التي يمكن معرفتها «غير» بالموصوف له بهذا الاعتبار. لكن إذا كانت تلك الصفة لازمة له وهو لازم لها، لم يكن في ذلك ما يكون أحدهما مفتقراً إلى الآخر ، مفعولاً للآخر، ولا علة فاعلة، ولا غائية، ولا صُورية. أكثر ما في ذلك أن تكون الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات افتقار الحال إلى محله القابل له . وهم يسمون القابل علة قابلة لكن فيما يحدث لها من المقبولات، لا فيما يكون لازماً لها أزلاً وأبداً. وإن قدر أنهم يسمون جميع ذلك «علة» و «معلولاً»، فتكون الذات علة قابلة للصفة بهذا الاعتبار. وكون الصفة «معلولة) هو معنى كونها صفة قائمة بالموصوف .
هذا فليس مجموع الذات والصفة مفتقراً إلى شيء من أنواع العلل الأربع، لا افتقار الحال إلى الفاعل ولا إلى الغاية ولا إلى القابل، ولا إلى الصورة فبطل أن يقال : المجموع مفتقر إلى جزئه افتقار المعلول إلى علته بوجه من الوجوه، لكن غايته أن فيه افتقار الصفة إلى الموصوف افتقار الحال اللازم لمحله إلى محله المستلزم له. فيقال لهم : وأي شيء في كونه موجوداً بنفسه لا فاعِل له ما يوجب نفي هذا التلازم الذي سموه «افتقاراً» نحو ذاته وصفاته؟

Third Station - The proof of the negators of attributes and the refutation of it.
... to its parts, and its part is other than it. So 'The composite is in need of something else, and that which is in need of something else is not necessary by itself.'
So it is said: The parts of this proof and its expressions which you call 'definitions' are all ambiguous expressions that bear truth and falsehood, and using ambiguous expressions in definitions and syllogism is a kind of sophistry.
So it is said to you: It is known that the term 'composite' is ambiguous, and what is meant by it here is 'an essence to which need pertains, which is attributes.' And thus what is meant by 'need' is the inseparability of essence and attributes, meaning that the essence does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable attribute, and the attribute does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable essence. And if it were assumed that by 'composition' is meant composition from sensory or intellectual parts with the inseparability of parts, then this is its meaning.
So if it is said: Every composite is in need of its part, if what is meant is that it entails its part, and that it has no existence except with the existence of its part, then this is correct. For the existence of the whole without each of its individuals is impossible. But if what is meant is that it is in need of it with the need of the effect for its agent, and the effect for its efficient cause, or for the receptive [cause], or the final [cause], or the formal [cause], then this is false, for the one among ten and the part of the whole cannot be an agent, nor a final cause, nor a locus for something else, nor is it the form.
Then your statement 'and its part is other than it' – it is said: The term 'other' refers to what is separate from the thing, and what can be separated from it, and what is not identical to it. If you mean that the part of the whole is separate from it, that is false, for it is impossible for it to be separate from it while being a part of it; thus it is impossible for it to be 'other' to it in this sense. And if you say: It can be separated from it, then this is not granted absolutely, but it is permissible in some individuals that it separates from other parts, and separates from the whole which is the social form. And that does not necessarily apply to every composite, especially according to their own principle. For the celestial sphere according to them is 'composite' from its parts and attributes, and it is not permissible according to them for its parts to disperse.
And the Muslims and the majority of rational people [hold] that God is living, knowing, powerful; and it is not permissible that God's attributes separate from His being living, knowing, powerful; rather He has never ceased and will never cease to be so. And His being living, knowing, powerful are necessary concomitants of His essence, and they are inseparable from His essence, and separation is not permissible upon them in any way. Thus it is impossible for these attributes to be 'others' in this sense.
And if 'the two others' is interpreted as that one of them is not the other, or as that it is permissible to know one without knowledge of the other, then there is no doubt that the attribute of the described that can be known is 'other' than the described in this sense. But when that attribute is necessary for him and he is necessary for it, there is not in that what makes one of them in need of the other, an effect of the other, nor an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a formal cause. The most there is in that is that the attribute is in need of the essence with the need of a state for its receptive locus. And they call the receptive cause a material cause, but in what occurs to it of received things, not in what is necessary for it eternally and perpetually. And if it is assumed that they call all that 'cause' and 'effect', then the essence would be a receptive cause for the attribute in this sense. And the attribute being an 'effect' is the meaning of its being an attribute subsisting in the described.
So this: the whole of essence and attribute is not in need of any of the four types of causes, neither the need of the state for the agent, nor for the final cause, nor for the receptive cause, nor for the formal cause. So it is invalid to say: The whole is in need of its part with the need of the effect for its cause in any way, but its utmost is that there is in it the need of the attribute for the described, the need of the necessary state for its locus that entails it. So it is said to them: What is there in His being existent by Himself with no agent for Him that necessitates negating this inseparability which they called 'need' regarding His essence and attributes?
And their statement: 'What is in need of something else is not necessary by itself' – it is said to them: It is known that what is meant by 'need' is inseparability; and what is meant by 'other' is what is included in the whole, either the essence or the attributes, not what is separate from it and what can be separated from it. And its utmost is that what is intended is that the attribute cannot do without the described. So what is meant is not the need of the effect for its efficient cause. And then there is not in this inseparability which you have called 'need' nor in these attributes which you have called 'others' what necessitates that any of that be an effect of an agent nor of an efficient cause.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of a standard logical proof used by those who deny that God has real attributes (the 'negators of attributes'). The proof states: 'Every composite is in need of something else, and that which is in need of something else is not necessary by itself. Therefore, if God had attributes, He would be composite and thus not necessary by Himself.' Ibn Taymiyya argues that this proof rests on equivocal terms, and thus is sophistical.

He begins by identifying the key terms in the argument: 'composite' (murakkab), 'need' (iftiqar), and 'other' (ghayr). He claims each is ambiguous (mujmal), capable of bearing both true and false meanings. The logicians' use of such terms in definitions and syllogisms, he says, is sophistry (safsata).

He then clarifies what the proof should mean if it is to be valid. The intended meaning of 'composite' here is not the usual sense of parts externally assembled, but rather 'an essence to which need pertains, which is attributes'. In other words, the 'composition' is between the essence (dhat) and its attributes (sifat). Correspondingly, 'need' here does not mean dependence on a separate external cause; it means the inseparability (talazum) of essence and attribute: the essence cannot exist without its attribute, and the attribute cannot exist without its essence. This is a mutual entailment, not a causal dependence.

Ibn Taymiyya then systematically examines the premises. Considering the statement 'Every composite is in need of its part', he distinguishes two possible interpretations. If it means the composite entails its part and cannot exist without it, then it is true—the whole indeed cannot exist without its parts. But if it means the composite depends on its part as an effect depends on its efficient, final, formal, or material cause, then it is false. A part of a whole cannot be an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a locus, nor a form. Thus the crucial sense of 'need' in the refuted proof is invalid.

He then turns to 'its part is other than it'. The term 'other' can mean 'separate' (mubayin), i.e., distinct and capable of separation. He argues that a part cannot be separate from the whole it constitutes, so it is not 'other' in that sense. Alternatively, if they mean it can be separated, that is not universally true; in some composites separation is impossible, such as the celestial spheres (according to the philosophers themselves) and especially in God's attributes, which are eternal and inseparable. Muslims hold that God is eternally living, knowing, powerful, and these attributes are necessary concomitants (lawazim) of His essence; they cannot separate. Thus they are not 'others' in the sense of being separable.

There is another valid interpretation of 'other': two things are 'other' if one is not identical to the other, or if knowledge of one does not entail knowledge of the other. In this sense, an attribute is indeed 'other' than the essence. But this kind of 'otherness' does not imply dependence of the efficient-cause type. The attribute's only 'need' is that it subsists in the essence as a state (hal) in its locus (mahall), but this is a necessary inseparability, not a causal relation. Even if one calls this 'cause and effect' (the essence being a receptive cause), that does not entail that the composite (essence+attributes) is in need of an external agent.

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the composite of essence and attributes is not in need of any of the four causes (efficient, final, material, formal). The utmost relationship is the attribute's need for the essence as a necessary state for its locus. This does not conflict with God being self-sufficient (wajib al-wujud bi-dhatihi). The logicians' argument fails because they equivocate on 'need' and 'other'. The true meaning of 'need' here is simply inseparability, and 'other' means a part of the whole, not something external. Thus nothing in this entails that God is not necessary by Himself.

Key Terms
المركب (al-murakkab): Here, 'composite' does not mean physical or logical parts; it refers to an essence that has attributes, i.e., the combination of essence and attributes. [Note: In standard logic, murakkab means any compound entity; here it is specifically applied to the God-attributes relationship.]
الافتقار (al-iftiqar): In this context, 'need' means mutual inseparability (talazum) between essence and attribute, not causal dependence on an external agent. [Note: Usually iftiqar means dependence on a cause; here it is redefined as mere entailment.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does the logicians' proof fall into sophistry?
A) Because it uses incorrect syllogistic figures
B) Because its key terms are ambiguous and can bear both true and false meanings
C) Because it denies divine attributes entirely
D) Because it relies on the four causes

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous terms in any logical argument must be disambiguated before validity can be assessed; otherwise the argument is sophistical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the ambiguous terms in the following argument: 'Everything that is composed of parts is temporally originated; the world is composed of parts; therefore the world is temporally originated.' Which terms could be ambiguous? How would Ibn Taymiyya critique this?
?
Choose one of the three terms (composite, need, other) and write a brief disambiguation showing the different meanings that could be intended in a theological context.
